Definition of Existence: Ash'ari and Razi's Views
Bajuri defines wujud as a state and presents the two opinions of Ash'ari and Razi, with marginal commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main opinions on the nature of existence as a divine attribute and the definition of existence as a state necessary for the essence.

Core Idea
Existence is foundational, yet scholars disagree whether it is identical to the existent or a distinct state.

In this passage, Bajuri explains why existence is the first attribute discussed and presents the key disagreement between al-Ash'ari and al-Razi. You will see how the well-known definition of existence as a 'state necessary for the essence' is built on Razi's view, and how the marginal commentary clarifies the nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation to get the overall flow.
Notice the author's concise style and the two quoted opinions.
2
Identify the two opinions and the definition
Find where the text says 'it is said: it is identical to the existent' and 'it is said: it is other than the existent'. Then note the well-known definition that follows.
The definition is based on Razi's view.
3
Understand why existence is like an origin
The text explains that existence is presented first because judgment of other attributes depends on affirming existence. Reflect on why it says 'like the origin' not 'origin'.
The marginal commentary clarifies that it is not a true origin because all attributes are eternal.
4
Explore the commentary on 'state' and 'as long as the essence'
Read the commentary explaining the definition: existence is a state intermediate between non-existence and existence, and the condition 'as long as the essence' is explained. Understand how this applies to temporal vs eternal existence.
The commentary addresses potential misunderstandings about the condition.
5
Reflect on why the text says 'like the origin' not 'origin'
The final part of the commentary shows that if existence were a true origin, other attributes would be temporal, contradicting their eternity. Discuss why the phrasing matters.
This highlights a deep theological point about the co-eternity of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي الوجود إلخ. إنما قدم الوجود على غيره; لأنه كالأصل لما عداه; إذ لا يصح الحكم بالقدم وما بعده إلا بعد ثبوته. واختلف في الوجود; فقيل: هو عين الموجود، وهذا القول لأبي الحسن الأشعري. وقيل: هو غير الموجود، وهذا القول للإمام الرازي، وعليه التعريف المشهور ، وهو أنه الحال الواجبة للذات ما دامت الذات حال كون تلك الحال غير معللة بعلة. (قوله: إذ لا يصح الحكم... إلخ) أي: لا يتأتى اعتقاد ثبوت القدم لله إلا مع وجوده، بخلاف اعتقاد وجوده فإنه يمكن بدون اعتقاد بقية الصفات، فالحكم بالصفات متوقف على الحكم به، والحكم به لا يتوقف على الحكم بغيره من الصفات، وإنما قال: كالأصل; لأنه ليس أصلاً حقيقة; لأن صفات الله لا ترتيب في ثبوتها له، بل هو متصف بها كلها أزلاً، والكلام في غير صفة الأفعال; لأنها حادثة. (قوله: وهو أنه الحال) أي: الصفة المتوسطة بين المعدوم والموجود كما قدمه، وهذا التعريف يشمل الوجود الحادث والقديم، وإدخالهما في تعريف واحد لا يضر; لأنه لا حد، والمراد بتلك الحال: حصول الذات وتحققها خارجاً بحيث تصح رؤيتها - أي: الذات، وأما التحقق نفسه; فلا تمكن رؤيته; لأن الفرض أنه حال، والأحوال لا تملك رؤيتها. (وقوله: ما دامت الذات) محتاج إليه بالنسبة للوجود الحادث; لأنه من الواجب المقيد بخلاف الوجود القديم، فليس محتاجاً إلى التقييد بدوام الذات، بل هو ضار لما فيه من إيهام انقطاع دوامها ويجاب بأن المقصود به التنبيه على أن الوجود لازم للذات نفسها لا لعلة قائمة بالذات، بخلاف الأحوال المعللة، فإنها لازمة لعللها. (قوله: كالأصل) لم يقل: أصل; لأن الوجود لو كان أصلاً حقيقةً; للزم حدوث بقية الصفات; لأن الأصل يتقدم على الفرع، وليس كذلك.
And it is existence etc. He only preceded existence over others because it is like the origin for what is other than it; for the judgment of pre-eternity and what follows it is not valid except after its affirmation. And they differed regarding existence; it is said: it is identical to the existent, and this is the saying of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. And it is said: it is other than the existent, and this is the saying of Imam al-Razi, and upon it is the well-known definition, which is that it is the state necessary for the essence as long as the essence exists, while that state is not caused by a cause. (His saying: 'for the judgment... etc.') means: the belief in the affirmation of pre-eternity for God is not attainable except with His existence, unlike the belief in His existence, for it can be without believing in the rest of the attributes. So the judgment of attributes depends on the judgment of Him, and the judgment of Him does not depend on the judgment of other attributes. He only said 'like the origin' because it is not truly an origin; for the attributes of God have no order in their affirmation for Him, but He is described by all of them eternally, and the discussion is not about attributes of acts because they are temporal. (His saying: 'and it is that it is the state') i.e. the attribute intermediate between the non-existent and the existent as he previously mentioned, and this definition includes temporal and eternal existence, and including both in one definition does not harm because it is not a strict definition (ḥadd). The meaning of that state is the obtaining of the essence and its realization externally such that its vision is valid – i.e. the essence; as for the realization itself, it cannot be seen because it is assumed to be a state, and states cannot be seen. (His saying: 'as long as the essence') is needed for temporal existence because it is of the contingent conditional, unlike eternal existence, so it does not need the restriction by the duration of the essence; rather it is harmful because it suggests the interruption of its duration. And it is answered that the intent of it is to indicate that existence is necessary for the essence itself, not for a cause subsisting in the essence, unlike caused states, for they are necessary for their causes. (His saying: 'like the origin') He did not say 'origin' because if existence were truly an origin, the rest of the attributes would be temporal, because the origin precedes the branch, and it is not so.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of existence (wujūd) as one of the divine attributes. Bajuri begins by explaining why wujūd is mentioned first in the text: it is like the origin (ka-al-aṣl) for the other attributes because one cannot judge pre-eternity or other attributes without first affirming existence. Then he presents two opinions: al-Ashʿarī holds that wujūd is identical to the existent (ʿayn al-mawjūd), while al-Rāzī holds it is other than the existent (ghayr al-mawjūd). The well-known definition, based on Rāzī’s view, is that wujūd is the state (ḥāl) necessary for the essence as long as the essence exists, and that state is not caused by a cause. The marginal commentary then unpacks each part. On 'for the judgment of pre-eternity...', Ajhūrī clarifies that belief in God’s pre-eternity requires belief in His existence, but belief in existence does not require belief in other attributes; so existence is like an origin in priority of judgment, but not a real origin because all divine attributes are eternal and there is no temporal sequence. On 'and it is that it is the state', Ajhūrī explains that this definition refers to the attribute intermediate between non-existent and existent, covering both temporal and eternal existence; it is a descriptive definition (rasm) rather than a strict definition (ḥadd). The state is the obtaining (ḥuṣūl) of the essence externally, making it possible to see the essence; but the 'obtaining' itself (the state) cannot be seen. On 'as long as the essence', Ajhūrī notes this condition is necessary for temporal existence, which is contingent, but for eternal existence it might wrongly suggest interruption; the answer is that the phrase emphasizes that existence is necessary for the essence itself, not for a cause. Finally, Anbābī comments on 'like the origin': the text says 'like the origin' not 'origin' because if existence were truly an origin, the other attributes would be temporal (since the origin precedes the branch), but they are all eternal.

Key Terms
الوجود (existence)
الحال (state)
عين الموجود (identical to the existent)
غير الموجود (other than the existent)
الحال الواجبة للذات (state necessary for the essence)
القدم (pre-eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence is just like any other divine attribute with no special priority, or that the definition of existence is a strict logical definition.
Correction: The passage shows that existence has a foundational role (like an origin) in the order of judgment, though not in temporal sequence. The definition of existence as a state is a descriptive definition (rasm), not a strict definition (ḥadd), and it covers both temporal and eternal existence.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why did the author mention existence first among the attributes?
A) Because it is the most important attribute.
B) Because it is like an origin for the others, as judgments of other attributes depend on affirming existence first.
C) Because it is the only attribute that is not a state.
D) Because it is the only attribute denied by the Mu'tazila.

Key Takeaway
The definition of existence as a non-caused state necessary for the essence is based on Razi's view, while Ash'ari saw existence as identical to the existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is the foundation for all other divine attributes, how does your understanding of God's existence shape your worship and reliance on Him? Do you truly live as though He exists, or is His existence a mere intellectual nod?
?
The scholars disagreed on whether existence is identical to the existent or a separate state. Does this debate affect your personal conviction about God's oneness? Are you comfortable with theological ambiguity in matters of faith, or does it unsettle you?
?
The passage states that belief in existence does not require belief in other attributes. Yet we often reduce our relationship with God to His attributes. Are you guilty of focusing on God's mercy or power while neglecting the raw reality of His existence? What would change if you meditated on His existence alone for a day?

Why Existence is Prioritized Among Divine Attributes
Al-Bajuri explains the priority of existence and clarifies it is not a real root.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Sanusi lists existence first among the divine attributes and why it is considered 'like the root' rather than an actual root.

Core Idea
Existence is prioritized as a conceptual starting point, not as a real source of other attributes.

Al-Bajuri explains that existence appears first in the list of divine attributes because judgments about other attributes—like pre-eternity—depend on first affirming existence. However, he carefully uses the phrase 'like the root' to show that this priority is only in the order of understanding, not in reality. The glosses by al-Ajhuri and al-Anbabi reinforce that all attributes are equally eternal and that existence is not a temporal origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the main argument and the comments by the two scholars.
2
Identify the reason for prioritizing existence
Notice that al-Bajuri says judgments of pre-eternity (qidam) and other attributes require existence to be affirmed first. This is a logical priority, not a temporal one.
3
Understand 'like the root'
Al-Sanusi says existence is 'like the root' (ka al-asl), not 'the root' itself. The glosses explain why: if it were a real root, the other attributes would be created in time, which is impossible because all attributes are eternal.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
This lesson shows that in kalam, the order of presentation does not imply actual priority in reality. Attributes are all equally eternal, and existence is simply the conceptual foundation for discussing them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَهِيَ الْوُجُودُ إلخ) إنما قَدَّمَ الوجودَ على غيره؛ لأنه كالأصل لما عداه؛ إذ لا يَصِحُ الحُكْمُ بالقِدَم وما بعده إلا بعد ثبوته.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري: (قوله: إِذْ لا يَصِحُ الْحُكْمُ... إلخ) أي: لا يتأتّى اعتقاد ثبوت القدم الله إلا مع ،وجوده، بخلاف اعتقاد وجوده فإنه يمكن بدون اعتقاد بقية الصفات، فالحكم بالصفات متوقف على الحكم به، والحكم به لا يتوقف على الحكم بغيره من الصفات، وإنما قال: (كالأصل المفيد أنه ليس أصلاً حقيقة؛ لأن صفات الله لا ترتيب في ثبوتها له، بل هو متصف بها كلها أزلاً، والكلام في غير صفة الأفعال؛ لأنها حادثة.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي: (قوله: كَالْأَصْلِ) لم يقل : أصل؛ لأن الوجود لو كان أصلاً حقيقةً؛ للزم حدوث بقية الصفات؛ لأن الأصل يتقدَّمُ على الفرع، وليس كذلك.
(His saying: 'And they are existence, etc.') He only prioritized existence over others because it is like the root (asl) for what is other than it; since the judgment of pre-eternity (qidam) and what follows it is not valid except after its affirmation. Marginal commentary of al-Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'Since the judgment is not valid... etc.') i.e., the belief in the affirmation of pre-eternity for God is not attainable except with His existence, unlike the belief in His existence, which is possible without belief in the remaining attributes. Thus, the judgment of attributes depends on the judgment of it, and the judgment of it does not depend on the judgment of other attributes. He only said: ('like the root') indicating that it is not a real root; because God's attributes have no order in their affirmation for Him, rather He is described by all of them eternally, and the discussion is other than the attribute of actions because they are originated. Taqrirat of al-Allama Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His saying: 'like the root') He did not say 'a root' because if existence were a real root, then the remaining attributes would be originated, since the root precedes the branch, and that is not the case.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on al-Sanusi's prioritization of existence. Al-Bajuri begins by stating the reason: existence is presented first because it is like the root (asl) for all other attributes, as judgments of pre-eternity and other attributes depend on the prior affirmation of existence. Al-Ajhuri then clarifies that belief in God's pre-eternity cannot be attained without first believing in His existence, whereas belief in existence can stand alone. He adds that al-Sanusi's phrase 'like the root' implies it is not a real root, because God's attributes are all eternal without temporal priority; the discussion excludes attributes of action (which are originated). Al-Anbabi reinforces this: if existence were a true root, the other attributes would be created in time, since a root precedes its branch – but this is not the case. Thus, existence is prioritized only in the order of conception, not in reality.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
قدم (qidam)
أصل (asl)
صفات الله (sifat Allah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think existence is actually the source or basis from which other attributes derive, implying that other attributes are originated.
Correction: The passage clarifies that existence is only 'like the root' in the order of conception; all attributes are equally eternal and no attribute is temporally prior to another.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does al-Sanusi present existence before other attributes?
A) Because existence is the most important attribute.
B) Because judgments of pre-eternity and other attributes depend on affirming existence first.
C) Because existence is the only attribute that is eternal.
D) Because the other attributes are derived from existence.

Key Takeaway
Existence is prioritized only in the order of understanding, not in reality, because all attributes are eternal and no attribute temporally originates from another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat God's existence as a mere mental starting point, or does it truly anchor your understanding of all His other attributes? This week, begin your reflection on any attribute by first affirming, 'He exists.'
?
If existence were a real root, other attributes would be created. Have you ever unintentionally thought of God's mercy or power as 'coming after' His existence? Commit to affirming that all His attributes are equally eternal.
?
The passage says belief in existence can occur without belief in other attributes. Is your faith in God's existence steady even when you struggle to feel His mercy or justice? How can you strengthen that foundational certainty today?

Two Opinions on Existence and Its Definition as a State
Al-Bajuri reports two views on existence and defines it as an uncaused state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main positions on the relationship between existence and the existent, and the definition of existence as a state required by the essence.

Core Idea
Existence is a necessary state of the essence, not identical to it.

Al-Bajuri begins by reporting two classical positions on existence: al-Ash'ari's identification of existence with the existent, and al-Razi's distinction between them. He then adopts al-Razi's definition of existence as a state (hal) that necessarily belongs to the essence. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
Read the passage and locate the two views: one from al-Ash'ari and one from al-Razi. Note how each defines the relationship between existence (wujud) and the existent (mawjud).
Al-Ash'ari says existence is identical to the existent; al-Razi says it is other than it.
2
Understand the definition as a state
Examine al-Razi's definition: existence is a state (hal) necessary for the essence as long as the essence endures, and it is not caused by any cause. Consider what 'state' means here.
Al-Ajhuri explains that a state is intermediate between existent and non-existent.
3
Reflect on the clarifications
Read al-Ajhuri's commentary on why the definition applies to both originated and eternal existence, why states cannot be seen, and the purpose of the condition 'as long as the essence remains'.
This clarifies that existence is necessary for the essence itself, not for a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف في الوجود؛ فقيل: هو عين الموجود، وهذا القول لأبي الحسن الأَشْعَرِي. وقيل: هو غيرُ الموجود، وهذا القولُ للإمام الرازي، وعليه التعريف المشهورُ ، وهو أنه الحالُ الواجبة للذات ما دَامَتِ الذاتُ حالَ كَوْنِ تلك الحال غيرَ معللة بعلة.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري: (قوله: وَهُوَ أَنَّهُ الْحَالُ أي: الصفة المتوسطة بين المعدوم والموجود كما قدمه، وهذا التعريف يشمل الوجود الحادث والقديم، وإدخالهما في تعريف واحد لا يضر؛ لأنه لا حد، والمراد بتلك الحال: حصول الذات وتحققها خارجاً بحيث تصح رؤيتها - أي: الذات ، وأما التحقق نفسه؛ فلا تمكن رؤيته؛ لأن الفرض أنه حال، والأحوال لا تمكن رؤيتها.
(وقوله: مَا دَامَتِ الذَّاتُ) محتاج إليه بالنسبة للوجود الحادث؛ لأنه من الواجب المقيد بخلاف الوجود القديم، فليس محتاجاً إلى التقييد بدوام الذات، بل هو ضار لما فيه من إيهام انقطاع دوامها ويجاب بأن المقصود به التنبيه على أن الوجود لازم للذات نفسها لا لعلة قائمة بالذات، بخلاف الأحوال المعللة، فإنها لازمة لِعِللها.
And they differed concerning existence: it was said that it is identical to the existent (ayn al-mawjud), and this is the saying of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. And it was said that it is other than the existent (ghayr al-mawjud), and this is the saying of Imam al-Razi, and upon it is the well-known definition, which is that it is the state (hal) necessarily belonging to the essence (dhat) as long as the essence remains, with the condition that that state is not caused by a cause. Marginal commentary of al-Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'And that it is the state') i.e., the attribute intermediate between the non-existent and the existent as he previously stated. And this definition includes originated and eternal existence, and including them in one definition does not harm because it is a definition (hadd). And what is meant by 'that state' is the attainment of the essence and its realization externally such that its vision is possible – i.e., the essence. As for the realization itself, its vision is not possible because it is hypothesized to be a state, and states cannot be seen. (And his saying: 'as long as the essence remains') is needed with respect to originated existence because it is of the contingent restricted (al-wajib al-muqayyad), unlike eternal existence, which does not need restriction by the continuance of the essence; rather it is harmful because it suggests the interruption of its continuity. And it is answered that the intent is to draw attention to the fact that existence is necessary for the essence itself, not for a cause subsisting in the essence, unlike caused states (al-ahwal al-mu'allala), which are necessary for their causes.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents a doctrinal definition and classification. Al-Bajuri first reports two historical positions on existence: (1) al-Ash'ari's view that existence is identical to the existent, and (2) al-Razi's view that existence is other than the existent, which is the basis for the well-known definition. Then he gives al-Razi's definition: existence is a state (hal) that necessarily belongs to the essence as long as the essence endures, and it is not caused by any cause. Al-Ajhuri explains that this definition applies to both originated and eternal existence, and it does not matter that the definition covers both because it is a technical definition (hadd), not a limiting definition. He further clarifies that the 'state' refers to the essence's external attainment and realization such that the essence itself can be seen, but the realization qua state cannot be seen because states are not visible. The condition 'as long as the essence remains' is necessary for originated existence (which is contingent), but for eternal existence it is not needed and might even be misleading. However, the purpose is to emphasize that existence is necessary for the essence itself, not for a cause, unlike caused states which depend on causes.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
موجود (mawjud)
حال (hal)
ذات (dhat)
معللة بعلة (mu'allala bi-illa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence as a 'state' means it is an additional attribute that can be perceived.
Correction: The passage defines existence as a state intermediate between existent and non-existent, and states that states cannot be seen. Existence is the essence's attainment and realization externally.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's definition, what does it mean for existence to be a 'state' (hal)?
A) It is an attribute that can be perceived by the senses.
B) It is an attribute that is intermediate between existence and non-existence and necessarily belongs to the essence.
C) It is a property that is caused by the essence.
D) It is identical to the essence itself.

Key Takeaway
Existence, according to the well-known definition, is a state necessarily inhering in the essence without being caused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence is a state that necessarily belongs to your essence, but it is not caused by any cause within you. Do you truly feel your dependence on the One who sustains your existence every moment, or do you take it for granted? Write one specific action you will take today to acknowledge this dependency.
?
The passage distinguishes between your existence (wujud) and you as an existent (mawjud). Have you ever considered that your identity is more than just your existence? What attributes of yours do you think are essential, and which are caused? How might this lesson reshape your self-understanding?
?
Al-Bajuri says existence is an intermediate state between non-existent and existent. Does this make your existence feel fragile? Let that awareness lead you to a sincere prayer of gratitude to the One who gives and sustains existence without any cause.

Classification of States into Caused and Uncaused
Al-Bajuri classifies states into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two kinds of states (ahwal) — those caused by a cause and those not — and their definitions according to Al-Bajuri.

Core Idea
States are either caused by a cause or uncaused.

Al-Bajuri begins by defining 'caused state' with clear examples like being powerful (caused by power) and being willing (caused by will). This leads to a simple but fundamental classification: some states are caused by something external to the essence, others are not. Understanding this distinction is key to later discussions about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the examples given for caused states.
The examples are 'being powerful' and 'being willing'.
2
Identify the two kinds
Find where Al-Bajuri explicitly states that states are of two kinds: uncaused and caused.
He says 'fa‛ulima min dhālika anna al-ḥāla qismān'.
3
Understand the definition
Note that a caused state is defined as being 'other than the essence' (ghayr al-dhāt).
4
Reflect on examples
Consider why 'being powerful' is caused by power, and what it means for a state to be uncaused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخرج بذلك الحالُ المعللة بعلة، كالكون قادراً، فإنه معلل بعلةٍ، وكالكون مُرِيداً، فإنه معلَّل بعلة، وهي الإرادة، وهكذا، ومعنى كونها معللةً بعلةٍ: أنها آخر غير الذات، فعُلِمَ من ذلك أن الحال قسمان: أحدهما: غير معللة بعلةٍ، والآخرُ معلَّل بعلةٍ، وعَدُّ الوجود صِفَةٌ على القول الأول غير ظاهر؛ لأن الصفةَ لازمة لشيء لا بُدَّ أن تكون غير الموصوف، إلا أن يُقَال: لما صح أن يُقال: الله موجود كما صَحْ أن يقال: الله عالِمٌ مثلاً ؛ سَاغَ عدُّ الوجود حينئذٍ صِفَةٌ لشبهه بها في ذلك.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري: (قوله: وَخَرَجَ بِذَلِكَ الْحَالُ... إلخ) أي: سواءً كانت حادثة ككون زيد أبيض اللازم لبياضه، أو قديمةً ككون الله قادراً اللازم لقدرته.
(قوله: إلا أَنْ يُقَالَ حاصله: أن يشبه الوجود بنحو العلم في وقوعه صفة في اللفظ، وتستعار الصفة للوجود، فالصفة في كلامه مستعملة في حقيقتها، وهو ما عدا الوجود وفي مجازها، وهو الوجود، وقول الله موجود أي يطلق عليه لفظ الوجود، وليس المعنى: أنه موصوف بالوجود؛ لأن الفرض أن الوجود عين الذات لا وصف لها.
And by that is excluded the state caused by a cause, such as being powerful, for it is caused by a cause; and being willing, for it is caused by a cause, which is will; and so on. And the meaning of its being caused by a cause is that it is other than the essence. Thus it is known from this that the state is of two kinds: one: not caused by a cause, and the other: caused by a cause. And counting existence as a property (sifa) according to the first saying is not apparent, because a property is necessarily attached to something and must be other than the described, unless it is said: Since it is valid to say 'God exists' just as it is valid to say 'God is knowing', for example, then counting existence as a property becomes permissible due to its similarity to it in that regard. Marginal commentary of al-Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'And by that is excluded the state... etc.') i.e., whether it is originated like Zayd's being white which is necessary for his whiteness, or eternal like God's being powerful which is necessary for His power. (His saying: 'unless it is said') Its summary: that existence resembles knowledge and the like in occurring as a property in wording, and the term 'property' is borrowed for existence. So the term 'property' in his speech is used in its real sense for what is other than existence, and in its metaphorical sense for existence. And the saying 'God exists' means that the word existence is applied to Him, and the meaning is not that He is described by existence, because it is hypothesized that existence is identical to the essence, not a description of it.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage classifies states and addresses the terminology of 'property.' Al-Bajuri first defines caused states (al-ahwal al-mu'allala) using examples: 'being powerful' is caused by power, 'being willing' is caused by will, and so on. These states are other than the essence. Thus states are of two kinds: uncaused (like existence itself) and caused. Then he notes that calling existence a 'property' (sifa) on the view that existence is identical to the existent is problematic, because a property must be other than the subject it describes. However, it can be allowed metaphorically because we can say 'God exists' just as we say 'God is knowing' – the linguistic similarity permits the term. Al-Ajhuri clarifies that caused states can be either originated (like Zayd's whiteness) or eternal (like God's being powerful). He also explains that the phrase 'unless it is said' means existence is like knowledge in being used as a property in speech, but it is a metaphorical use; the real meaning is that existence is applied to God as a label, not as a separate attribute, because existence is identical to the essence.

Key Terms
حال (hal)
معللة بعلة (mu'allala bi-illa)
ذات (dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Bajuri, how many kinds of states are there?
A) Two: caused and uncaused
B) Three: essential, accidental, and relational
C) One: only caused states
D) Two: eternal and originated

Key Takeaway
States in kalam are classified as either caused (by an external cause) or uncaused, with caused states being additional to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Bajuri classifies states as either caused or uncaused. Think of a recent moment of gratitude or despair. Was it caused by something external, or did it arise from your essential self? How does identifying the cause change how you should respond to that state in your relationship with Allah?
?
This lesson says that states like 'being powerful' are caused by a cause (power) and are 'other than the essence.' If you truly internalised that God's attributes are not His essence but caused states (in the kalam sense), would it make your worship more focused on Allah Himself rather than on His attributes? What would change in your prayer if you pondered that?
?
The passage teaches that some states are uncaused. In your own life, what aspects of your faith do you think are uncaused—i.e., not dependent on your actions or circumstances? How can you strengthen those uncaused states so that your faith is not shaken by changing causes?

Is Existence a Property? The Metaphorical Use
Al-Bajuri discusses the permissibility of calling existence a property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence is only metaphorically called a property (sifa) and the reasoning behind this linguistic allowance.

Core Idea
Existence is not a real property because it is identical to the essence.

Al-Bajuri addresses a subtle terminological point: can we call existence (wujud) a 'property' (sifa)? He argues that on the view that existence is identical to the existent, it is not literally a property because a property must be distinct from the described. However, due to linguistic similarity, it is permissible to call it a property metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation, focusing on the phrase 'not apparent' and the exception 'unless it is said'.
Note that 'first saying' refers to the view that existence is identical to the essence.
2
Understand the problem
Why is calling existence a property 'not apparent'? Because a property must be 'other than the described'.
Existence is not other than the essence; it is the essence itself.
3
Analyze the solution
Al-Bajuri allows it metaphorically because we say 'God exists' just like we say 'God is knowing'.
This is a linguistic analogy, not a theological claim of real property.
4
Reflect on the marginal commentary
Consider the commentary of al-Ajhuri, which clarifies that the term 'property' is used in both real and metaphorical senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage classifies states and addresses the terminology of 'property.' ?

?
Consider a moment when you reduced your identity to a set of attributes—your job, knowledge, or kindness—rather than your fundamental existence before Allah. How can the lesson that existence is not a property but your essence reshape your repentance and reliance on Allah's mercy alone?

Existence as a Mental Consideration
Al-Bajuri endorses the view that existence is an i'tibari matter, not an additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the correct interpretation of existence is as an i'tibari matter (mental consideration) that is objectively real yet not an additional visible attribute.

Core Idea
Existence is real but not a visible extra attribute—it is a mental consideration.

In this concluding passage, al-Bajuri presents the correct interpretation (al-haqq) that resolves the earlier ambiguity about whether existence is other than the existent. He endorses the view of al-Sa'd al-Taftazani that existence is not an additional thing that could be seen but rather a mental consideration (amr i'tibari). The marginal commentary by al-Ajhuri further clarifies how this understanding aligns with the definition of state and provides proof that existence is real.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that al-Bajuri says 'the truth is its interpretation' to the first view. Underline what he states as the correct understanding: existence is not additional but a mental consideration.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بل هو أمر اعتباري'.
2
Understand the term 'amr i'tibari'
Notice the explanation that it is fixed in itself irrespective of the mind, but cannot be seen. Contrast with the idea of being merely mental. Understand that it is real but not an extra entity.
The passage says 'ثابت في نفسه بقطع النظر عن الذهن'.
3
Examine the proof that existence is real
The passage states that proofs are established upon it, indicating it is a real belief. Reflect on why this is important for theology—proofs only apply to real things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا كلُّهُ بناءً على إبقاء الأول على ظاهره والحق تأويله كما قال السعد وغيره من المحققين بأن المراد أنه ليس أمراً زائداً على الموجود بحيث يُرى، بل هو أمرٌ اعتباري.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري: وعلى هذا فقوله: (حال) كون تلك الحال... إلخ) أتى به زيادة في الإيضاح، ولكون الإخراج به أظهر من الإخراج بقوله: (ما دامت الذات).
(قوله: بَلْ هُوَ أَمْرُ اعْتِبارِي) أي: ثابت في نفسه بقطع النظر عن الذهن، لكن لا تمكن رؤيته، فهذا القول في المعنى كقول الرازي () ، إلا أن الرازي يسمّي الوجود حالاً، والسعد ومن تبعه يسمونه أمراً اعتبارياً، وعلى كلا القولين فهو ثابت في الواقع مع عدم إمكان رؤيته، ومما يدل على أن الوجود على هذا القول حاصل في الواقع بقطع النظر عن الذهن: إقامة البرهان عليه؛ لأن البراهين إنما تقام على العقائد وهي لا تكون إلا واقعية.
And all of this is based on taking the first [view] at its apparent meaning, but the truth is its interpretation, as al-Sa'd and other investigators said, that what is meant is that it is not an additional thing over the existent such that it is seen, rather it is a mental consideration (amr i'tibari). Marginal commentary of al-Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: And based on this, his saying: 'state, with the condition that that state is not caused by a cause, etc.' came as an additional clarification, and because the exclusion [of caused states] by it is more apparent than the exclusion by his saying 'as long as the essence remains'. (His saying: 'rather it is a mental consideration') i.e., it is fixed in itself irrespective of the mind, but its vision is not possible. So this saying is in meaning the same as al-Razi's saying, except that al-Razi calls existence a state, while al-Sa'd and those who follow him call it a mental consideration. And on both sayings, it is fixed in reality while its vision is impossible. And among what indicates that existence on this saying occurs in reality irrespective of the mind is the establishment of a proof for it, because proofs are only established for beliefs, and beliefs are only real.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion by presenting the correct interpretation. Al-Bajuri states that the previous classification and discussion were based on taking the first view (that existence is other than the existent) at face value, but the truth (al-haqq) is the interpretation given by al-Sa'd al-Taftazani and other verifiers: existence is not an additional thing over the existent that could be seen; rather it is a mental consideration (amr i'tibari). Al-Ajhuri then comments that based on this, al-Sanusi's addition of 'with the condition that that state is not caused by a cause' in the definition serves as extra clarification and is more effective in excluding caused states than the phrase 'as long as the essence remains'. He then explains that 'mental consideration' means it is objectively real independent of the mind, but cannot be seen. This aligns with al-Razi's view in meaning, though al-Razi called it a 'state' while al-Sa'd calls it a 'mental consideration'. Both agree it is real in the external world yet not visible. The proof of its reality is that logical demonstrations are constructed upon it, and demonstrations only address real beliefs.

Key Terms
أمر اعتباري (amr i'tibari)
حال (hal)
وجود (wujud)
موجود (mawjud)
برهان (burhan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: 'Mental consideration' (amr i'tibari) means existence is merely mental or subjective.
Correction: The passage explicitly states it is 'fixed in itself irrespective of the mind' (thabit fi nafsihi bi-qat' al-nazar 'an al-dhihn) and that proofs are established upon it, indicating it is real and objective.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'amr i'tibari' mean?
A) A purely mental concept with no reality outside the mind.
B) Something that is fixed in itself irrespective of the mind but cannot be seen.
C) An additional attribute that can be seen.
D) A caused state that depends on a cause.

Key Takeaway
Existence is a real mental consideration (amr i'tibari) that is not an additional visible attribute but is proven by theological demonstrations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is not a visible attribute but a mental consideration that is proven by rational proofs, then what is the basis of your certainty that you exist? Do you rely on your senses or on deeper reasoning?
?
The passage says proofs are only established for real beliefs. Think of a core belief you hold about Allah. Is it based on a rational proof you can articulate, or is it just inherited? If you cannot produce a proof, is that belief truly real to you?
?
You live as if the physical world is the only reality. But this lesson teaches that existence itself is not seen—it is known through proof. How does this change the way you value intellectual certainty over sensory experience in your faith?

Division of States and the Status of Existence
Al-Bajuri divides states into two kinds and discusses existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the division of states into two kinds and why the status of existence as an attribute is debated and resolved.

Core Idea
A state is either caused by a cause or not; existence is a special case.

Al-Bajuri explores the nature of states (aḥwāl) and their relationship to divine attributes. He clarifies that states can be divided into those that are caused by a cause (like being powerful) and those that are not (like existence on one view). However, counting existence as an attribute raises a problem because an attribute must be distinct from the essence. This lesson unpacks that problem and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'state'
Read the passage and note how a state is defined: it is caused by a cause and is distinct from the essence. Examples like being powerful (caused by power) and being willing (caused by will) illustrate this.
The state is not the cause itself but the condition caused by the cause.
2
Identify the two divisions of states
The passage divides states into two kinds: those not caused by a cause and those caused by a cause. Find this division in the text.
This division is key to understanding the subsequent discussion.
3
Analyze the problem with existence as an attribute
The passage raises a difficulty: if existence is considered a state not caused by a cause, then it cannot be an attribute because attributes must be other than the described. Consider why this is problematic.
The solution is to treat existence as an attribute only by analogy with real attributes like knowledge.
4
Recognize the correct interpretation
The passage concludes that the truth is to interpret the first view metaphorically: existence is not an additional attribute but a considerational matter (amr iʿtibārī). Understand why this resolves the issue.
This aligns with the views of verifiers like al-Sa'd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي القدرة
وخرج بذلك الحالُ المعللة بعلة، كالكون قادراً، فإنه معلل بعلةٍ، وكالكون مُرِيداً، فإنه معلَّل بعلة، وهي الإرادة، وهكذا، ومعنى كونها معللةً بعلةٍ: أنها آخر غير الذات، فعُلِمَ من ذلك أن الحال قسمان: أحدهما: غير معللة بعلةٍ، والآخرُ معلَّل بعلةٍ، وعَدُّ الوجود صِفَةٌ على القول الأول غير ظاهر؛ لأن الصفةَ لازمة لشيء لا بُدَّ أن تكون غير الموصوف، إلا أن يُقَال: لما صح أن يُقال: الله موجود كما صَحْ أن يقال: الله عالِمٌ مثلاً ; سَاغَ عدُّ الوجود حينئذٍ صِفَةٌ لشبهه بها في ذلك.
وهذا كلُّهُ بناءً على إبقاء الأول على ظاهره والحق تأويله كما قال السعد وغيره من المحققين بأن المراد أنه ليس أمراً زائداً على الموجود بحيث يُرى، بل هو أمرٌ اعتباري.
And it is power. And by that is excluded the state caused by a cause, like being powerful, for it is caused by a cause, and like being willing, for it is caused by a cause, which is will, and so on. And the meaning of its being caused by a cause is that it is other than the essence. So it is known from that that the state is of two kinds: one not caused by a cause, and the other caused by a cause. And counting existence as an attribute on the first view is not apparent, because the attribute must be other than the described, unless it is said: since it is valid to say 'God exists' just as it is valid to say 'God is knowing', for example, then counting existence as an attribute at that time becomes permissible due to its similarity to it in that respect. And all of this is based on keeping the first upon its apparent meaning, while the truth is its interpretation, as al-Sa'd and other verifiers said, that what is meant is that it is not an additional matter to the existent such that it is seen, but rather it is a considerational matter.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines and expands on the concept of 'state' (ḥāl) and its division. The author begins by explaining that a 'state' is something that is not a cause but is caused by a cause, giving examples like being powerful (caused by power) and being willing (caused by will). The key is that such a state is distinct from the essence. From this, we learn that states are of two types: those not caused by a cause (like existence on one view) and those caused by a cause (like attributes). Then the author addresses a problem: if we take the first view (that states are not caused), counting existence as an attribute is problematic because an attribute must be other than the essence. The solution presented is that existence is called an attribute only by analogy with real attributes like knowledge, because we say 'God exists' just as we say 'God is knowing'. But this is only on the first view; the correct view is that existence is not an additional attribute but a considerational matter (amr iʿtibārī) as al-Sa'd and other verifiers hold. The passage thus clarifies the nature of existence in relation to the divine essence, a key point in kalām.

Key Terms
حال
صفة
وجود
أمر اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: States are merely mental constructs with no real existence.
Correction: In this context, states are real attributes (either caused by a cause or not) that are distinct from the essence. For example, being powerful is a real state caused by power.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is counting existence as an attribute not apparent on the first view?
A) Because existence is not a real attribute.
B) Because an attribute must be other than the described, and existence is not separate from the essence.
C) Because existence is a cause, not a state.
D) Because the first view is invalid.

Key Takeaway
States are divided into those caused and those not caused by a cause; existence is not a real attribute but a considerational matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is not a real attribute added to God but merely a considerational matter, does that change how you perceive God's reality? How does this affect your certainty that God's existence is not something that can be gained or lost?
?
You often speak of God as 'the Existent'. This lesson teaches that existence is not like power or will. Does this make you more aware that God's essence is simple and not composed of parts? How should this deepen your tawḥīd in your daily worship?
?
The author says counting existence as an attribute is only permissible by analogy. Are there other ways you might inadvertently attribute something to God incorrectly? Pause and examine your own speech about God—are there phrases that imply God's existence is just another quality? How can you correct that?

Existence as a Considerational Matter
Al-Ajhuri explains al-Bajuri's division of states and the considerational nature of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theologians distinguish between 'state' and 'considerational matter' in describing existence, and how this resolves the problem of speaking of God's existence as an attribute without compromising divine simplicity.

Core Idea
Existence is real but not an attribute – it is a 'considerational matter' that cannot be perceived.

This passage from al-Ajhuri's commentary unpacks a delicate theological point: how can we say God 'exists' without implying a separate attribute? Al-Ajhuri explains that al-Bajuri's phrase 'a state being that state' clarifies the exclusion of states from the essence, and then delves into the debate between al-Razi and al-Sa'd over whether existence is a 'state' or a 'considerational matter'. The key is that existence is real – provable by logic – but not a perceivable quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from clarifying states to the metaphorical use of 'attribute' and finally to the nature of existence.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the exclusion of states
Al-Ajhuri explains that 'state' is excluded from the essence and can be temporal or eternal. Grasp why this distinction is important for avoiding associating change with God.
Zayd's whiteness is temporal; God's power is eternal.
3
Analyze the metaphor of existence as an attribute
The phrase 'unless it is said' introduces a key solution: we speak of 'existence' as an attribute only metaphorically. Understand that saying 'God exists' does not mean existence is a separate attribute but that the word is applied to Him.
This preserves divine simplicity.
4
Differentiate between 'state' and 'considerational matter'
Al-Ajhuri compares al-Razi's view (existence is a state) with al-Sa'd's (existence is a considerational matter). Both agree it is real but invisible. Note the proof: logical proofs only apply to real things.
Considerational does not mean imaginary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا فقوله: (حال) كون تلك الحال... إلخ) أتى به زيادة في الإيضاح، ولكون الإخراج به أظهر من الإخراج بقوله: (ما دامت الذات).
(قوله: وَخَرَجَ بِذَلِكَ الْحَالُ... إلخ) أي: سواءً كانت حادثة ككون زيد أبيض اللازم لبياضه، أو قديمةً ككون الله قادراً اللازم لقدرته.
(قوله: إلا أَنْ يُقَالَ حاصله: أن يشبه الوجود بنحو العلم في وقوعه صفة في اللفظ، وتستعار الصفة للوجود، فالصفة في كلامه مستعملة في حقيقتها، وهو ما عدا الوجود وفي مجازها، وهو الوجود، وقول الله موجود أي يطلق عليه لفظ الوجود، وليس المعنى: أنه موصوف بالوجود؛ لأن الفرض أن الوجود عين الذات لا وصف لها.
(قوله: بَلْ هُوَ أَمْرُ اعْتِبارِي) أي: ثابت في نفسه بقطع النظر عن الذهن، لكن لا تمكن رؤيته، فهذا القول في المعنى كقول الرازي () ، إلا أن الرازي يسمّي الوجود حالاً، والسعد ومن تبعه يسمونه أمراً اعتبارياً، وعلى كلا القولين فهو ثابت في الواقع مع عدم إمكان رؤيته، ومما يدل على أن الوجود على هذا القول حاصل في الواقع بقطع النظر عن الذهن: إقامة البرهان عليه؛ لأن البراهين إنما تقام على العقائد وهي لا تكون إلا واقعية.
(۱) هو الفخر الرازي، صاحب التفسير الكبير، وستأتي ترجمته ص۷۳.
And upon this, his saying: 'a state being that state... etc.' he brought it as an increase in clarification, and because the exclusion by it is more apparent than the exclusion by his saying 'as long as the essence exists.' His saying: 'And by that is excluded the state... etc.' means: whether it is temporal like Zayd's being white necessary for his whiteness, or eternal like God's being powerful necessary for His power. His saying: 'unless it is said' its summary: that existence is likened to knowledge in its occurrence as an attribute in wording, and the attribute is borrowed for existence, so the attribute in his speech is used in its real meaning, which is other than existence, and in its metaphorical meaning, which is existence. And the saying 'God exists' means that the word 'existence' is applied to Him, and the meaning is not that He is described by existence, because the assumption is that existence is identical to the essence, not an attribute of it. His saying: 'rather it is a considerational matter' means: it is established in itself regardless of the mind, but it cannot be seen. So this saying in meaning is like the saying of al-Razi, except that al-Razi calls existence a state, while al-Sa'd and those who follow him call it a considerational matter. In both sayings, it is established in reality while being impossible to see. And among what indicates that existence on this view is obtained in reality regardless of the mind is the establishment of the proof upon it, because proofs are only established for beliefs, and they are only real.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a teacher's (al-Ajhuri's) explanation of key phrases from al-Bajuri's text. It begins by clarifying that al-Bajuri's phrase 'a state being that state' was added for extra clarity, making the exclusion (of states from the essence) more apparent. Then it explains that the states excluded can be either temporal (like Zayd's whiteness caused by actual whiteness) or eternal (like God's being powerful caused by His eternal power). The most important part is the explanation of the phrase 'unless it is said': this is the solution to the problem of calling existence an attribute. The idea is that we use the word 'attribute' for existence metaphorically, not literally, because existence is not a separate attribute but identical to the essence. When we say 'God exists', we simply apply the word 'existence' to Him, not that He is described by an additional attribute. Finally, the phrase 'rather it is a considerational matter' is explained: existence is real (it is not a mere mental construct), but it cannot be perceived by the senses. Al-Ajhuri notes that this view is similar to al-Razi's, who calls existence a 'state', while al-Sa'd calls it a 'considerational matter'. Both agree it is real but invisible. The proof of its reality is that we can build logical proofs about it, and proofs only apply to real things.

Key Terms

صفة
وجود
أمر اعتباري
الرازي
السعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'considerational' means imaginary or non-existent.
Correction: Al-Ajhuri stresses that existence is real and established in reality, but it cannot be perceived by the senses. Proofs only apply to real things.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ajhuri, what is the difference between al-Razi's and al-Sa'd's views on existence?
A) Al-Razi denies existence; al-Sa'd affirms it.
B) Al-Razi calls existence a state; al-Sa'd calls it a considerational matter.
C) Al-Razi says existence is invisible; al-Sa'd says it is visible.
D) Al-Razi says existence is an attribute; al-Sa'd says it is not.

Key Takeaway
Existence is real and provable logically, but it is not an attribute that can be perceived; it is a considerational matter distinct from the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe in God's existence, but have you ever paused to consider that this belief rests on logical proof, not on seeing or feeling Him? How secure is your conviction when your senses offer no evidence?
?
If existence is real yet invisible, what does that say about your reliance on the physical world for certainty? Are there other truths you hold that cannot be seen but are more certain than what you see?
?
When doubts about God's existence arise, do you turn to proofs like those in this lesson, or do you rely on feelings? What would it take for you to internalize that proof is enough — that existence is real even when hidden?

The Two Meanings of Qidam: Divine and Created
The matn defines qidam for God and for others, with linguistic and technical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dual definition of qidam and how its literal and metaphorical usage differs between theologians and linguists.

Core Idea
Qidam means 'no beginning' for Allah, but 'old' for everything else.

This passage from Umm al-Barahin clarifies a crucial term in Islamic creed: qidam. While often translated simply as 'eternity,' the word has a precise theological meaning when applied to Allah—unqualified beginninglessness—and a different, relative meaning when applied to creatures. The passage also highlights a key difference in how theologians and linguists treat the term, which is essential for avoiding confusion in creedal discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the two distinct definitions given: one for Allah and one for others.
The passage uses two formulations for Allah's qidam: 'absence of a beginning of existence' and 'absence of commencement.'
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where the passage defines qidam for Allah (عَدَمُ أَوَّلِيَّةِ الْوُجُودِ) and for other than Allah (طُولُ الْمُدَّةِ). Notice the legal example with a slave to illustrate the non-divine meaning.
The example shows that 'qadim' can be quantified by a year in legal contexts.
3
Understand the terminological reversal
The passage contrasts the usage of theologians (المتكلمين) and linguists (اللغويين). Theologians take qidam literally for Allah and metaphorically for creatures; linguists do the opposite. Make sure you grasp this reversal.
This distinction prevents misinterpretation when reading works of kalam versus ordinary Arabic.
4
Reflect on the key takeaway
Ask yourself: why is it important that qidam for Allah means absolute beginninglessness? How might confusion arise if we mix the two senses?
This connects to the broader lesson on divine attributes: Allah's attributes are unique and not comparable to created qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقِدَمُ هو في حقه تعالى: عدمُ أَوَّلِيَّةِ الوجود، وإن شئت قلت: عدم افتتاح الوجود، وفي حقّ غيره - كما في قولهم: هذا بناء قديم -: طول المدة، وضُبِطَ بسنةٍ، فإذا قال: كلُّ من كان قديماً من عبيدي فهو حُرِّ؛ عَتَقَ مَنْ له عنده سَنَة. وهو في اصطلاح المتكلمين حقيقةً في الأول مجاز في الثاني، وفي اصطلاح اللغويين بالعكس.
And pre-eternity (al-qidam) with respect to Him, exalted, is the absence of a beginning of existence, and if you wish you may say: the absence of the commencement of existence. And with respect to other than Him - as in their saying: 'this is an ancient building' - it is length of duration, and it is defined by a year. So if he says: 'every one of my slaves who is old is free,' then the one who has had a year (in his service) is freed. And it is in the terminology of the theologians (mutakallimūn) literal in the first and metaphorical in the second, while in the terminology of the linguists it is the opposite.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage provides a definition and distinction for the term 'qidam' (pre-eternity or antiquity). It begins by defining qidam with respect to God: it means that His existence has no beginning. Two formulations are given: absence of an initial point (ʿadam awwaliyyat al-wujūd) or absence of commencement. For other than God, like an old building, qidam means long duration, and it is even defined legally as a period of one year (e.g., in the context of manumission of a slave who has been in service for a year). The passage then notes a terminological difference: theologians use 'qidam' literally for God (no beginning) and metaphorically for others, while linguists do the reverse, using it literally for old things and metaphorically for God. This distinction is important for understanding how terms are used in different disciplines and avoids confusion in theological discussions.

Key Terms
قِدَم
أولية الوجود
اصطلاح المتكلمين
اصطلاح اللغويين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qidam means the same for God and creatures: both refer to being old or ancient.
Correction: For God, qidam means absolute beginninglessness (no beginning at all), not just old age. For creatures, it means a long period of time, and the two usages are not equivalent because God's existence transcends time.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'qidam' mean in the terminology of the theologians (mutakallimūn) when applied to God?
A) Long duration.
B) Absence of a beginning of existence.
C) Being ancient in time.
D) Existence for more than a year.

Key Takeaway
Qidam for Allah means having no beginning, while for creatures it means long existence; theologians reserve the literal sense for Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Qadim,' do you mean He is simply 'very old' or do you truly affirm that He has no beginning? How would you correct someone who thinks of Allah's eternity as just an extremely long time?
?
The passage shows that the same word can have opposite literal meanings depending on context. Where in your own understanding of Allah's attributes might you be mixing up everyday language with precise theological definitions? What will you do to guard against that?
?
If the theologians reserve literal qidam for Allah alone, then every time you describe created things as 'old,' you are using the word metaphorically. Does this humbly remind you that only Allah is truly without beginning, and that everything else is contingent? How does that shape your worship?

The Permissibility of Calling Allah 'al-Qadim'
The matn and commentaries discuss the permissibility of calling Allah al-Qadim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the evidence from consensus and hadith that establishes the permissibility of using the name 'al-Qadim' for Allah, and how scholars resolved apparent contradictions.

Core Idea
Divine names are not limited to Qur'anic text; consensus and hadith can also establish them.

Is it permissible to call Allah by the name 'al-Qadim' (the Pre-eternal)? Some scholars hesitated because divine names are tawqīfī (based only on revelation). This lesson examines the matn's argument that it is permissible, supported by consensus and a hadith. We'll also see how commentators addressed an apparent contradiction in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the matn and the issue
Begin by reading the matn statement: 'And the correct view is that it is permissible to apply the term al-Qadim to Him... due to the establishment of that by consensus and its occurrence in some narrations.'
Notice the two proofs: consensus (ijma') and a hadith.
2
Understand the evidence
The matn presents consensus (ijma') and a narration (hadith) as proof. Consensus is from authoritative scholars, and the narration is from Ibn Majah's Sunan where al-Qadim is listed among the 99 names.
The term 'al-Awwal' is similar in meaning but more commonly used.
3
Examine the commentaries
Al-Ajhuri clarifies that the consensus is from those whose consensus counts. Al-Anbabi points out an apparent contradiction: if consensus already establishes permissibility, why say 'the correct view'? He explains it follows the phrasing of al-Sharqawi.
The contradiction is resolved by understanding that some scholars doubted, so the statement affirms the correct view.
4
Consider the hadith evidence
Al-‘Iraqi reports that al-Halimi counted al-Qadim among the names, citing the hadith. This shows the name is established by the Sunnah, even if not explicitly in the Qur'an.
5
Confirm the conclusion
Despite some hesitation due to the tawqifi nature of divine names, the correct view is permissibility. The consensus and hadith overcome the hesitation.
This settles the issue: al-Qadim is a permissible name for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصحيحُ أنه يجوز إطلاقُ القديم عليه تعالى؛ لثبوت ذلك بالإجماع، وورودِهِ في بعض الروايات بَدَلَ: الأول.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري .
(قوله: لِتُبُوتِ ذَلِكَ بِالإجماع أي بإجماع من يعتد بإجماعه، فالتأم مع ما قبله.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي.
(قوله: لِتُبُوتِ ذَلِكَ بِالْإِجْمَاعِ) أي: على وَجْهِ يُنْتِجُ الجواز بحيث لا يكون الإطلاق على سبيل المشاكلة مثلاً. وفيه: أنه إذا كان الإطلاق ثابتاً بالإجماع؛ فلا معنى للخلاف في جواز الإطلاق المشار إليه بقوله: (والصحيح... إلخ)، ففي عبارته تناف وتناقض، لكن هو تابع في ذلك لعبارة الشرقاوي على الهدهدي
وعبارة المصنف في شرحه: وهل يجوز أن يتلفظ بلفظ القديم في حقه تعالى، فيقال: هو قديم؛ لأن معناه واجب له جل وعز عقلاً ونقلاً، أو لا يتلفظ بذلك، وإنما يقال: يجب له تعالى القدمُ ونحو هذا من العبارات، ولا يُطْلَقُ عليه في اللفظ اسم القديم؛ لأن أسماءه جل وعزّ توقيفية؟ هذا مما تردَّدَ فيه بعض المشايخ، لكن قال العِرَاقِي في "شرح أصول الشبكي": عَدَّهُ الحَلِيمِيُّ من الشافعية في الأسماء، وقال: لم يَرِدْ في الكتاب نصاً، ولكن وَرَدَ في السنة، قال العراقي وأشار بذلك إلى ما رواه ابن مَاجَة في "سننه" من حديث أبي هريرة وفيه عَد القديم في التسعة والتسعين .اهـ.
And the correct view is that it is permissible to apply the term 'al-Qadim' to Him, exalted, due to the establishment of that by consensus (ijmāʿ), and its occurrence in some narrations in place of 'al-Awwal'.
Marginal note of the scholar Aḥmad al-Ajhurī: His saying: 'due to the establishment of that by consensus' means by the consensus of those whose consensus is considered, so it coheres with what preceded.
Notes of the scholar Muḥammad al-Anbābī: His saying: 'due to the establishment of that by consensus' means in a manner that yields permissibility, such that the application is not by way of simile (mushākala), for example. And there is an issue: if the application is established by consensus, then there is no meaning to the disagreement in the permissibility of the application indicated by his saying: 'and the correct view... etc.' So in his statement there is contradiction and inconsistency. But he follows in that the statement of al-Sharqāwī on al-Hudhudī, and the statement of the author in his commentary: 'Is it permissible to utter the word al-Qadīm with respect to Him, exalted, so that it is said 'He is Qadīm', because its meaning is obligatory for Him, glory be to Him, both rationally and transmitted? Or is it not uttered, and rather it is said: 'pre-eternity is obligatory for Him, exalted' and similar expressions, and the name al-Qadīm is not applied to Him verbally because His names, glory be to Him, are tawqīfī (based on revelation)? This is something about which some shaykhs have hesitated. But al-ʿIrāqī in his commentary on 'Uṣūl al-Shubukī' said: Al-Ḥalīmī among the Shāfiʿīs counted it among the names, and said: It has not come in the Book as a text, but it has come in the Sunnah. Al-ʿIrāqī said, indicating thereby the ḥadīth narrated by Ibn Mājah in his Sunan from Abū Hurayra in which al-Qadīm is counted among the ninety-nine (names). End.'
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific theological and juristic issue: Is it permissible to call Allah by the name 'al-Qadīm' (the Pre-eternal)? The matn states that the correct view is that it is permissible, supported by consensus (ijmāʿ) and because the term appears in some narrations as a substitute for 'al-Awwal' (the First).

Al-Ajhuri's marginal note simply clarifies that the consensus referred to is that of the scholars whose consensus is authoritative.

Al-Anbabi's note provides a more detailed critique and explanation. He points out an apparent contradiction in the matn: if the permissibility of using 'al-Qadīm' is established by consensus, then why is there a separate statement 'the correct view' as if there were a disagreement? He resolves this by noting that the author of the commentary (likely al-Bajuri or another) is following the phrasing of al-Sharqāwī on al-Hudhudī and the author's own commentary where he presents the issue as a matter of scholarly dispute: some scholars hesitate because divine names are tawqīfī (only to be used if revealed in the Qur'an or authentic hadith). However, al-‘Irāqī reports that al-Ḥalīmī, a Shāfi‘ī scholar, included 'al-Qadīm' among the ninety-nine names, citing a hadith from Ibn Mājah. So despite some hesitation, the use is supported by consensus and hadith.

Thus, the passage concludes that using 'al-Qadīm' for Allah is permissible, and the apparent contradiction is resolved by understanding that the statement 'the correct view' refers to the outcome after considering the arguments.

Key Terms
قديم
إطلاق
إجماع
توقيفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that divine names must only come from explicit Qur'anic text.
Correction: The passage shows that consensus and Sunnah can also establish a name like 'al-Qadim'.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence given in the matn for the permissibility of using the name 'al-Qadim' for Allah?
A) It is mentioned in the Qur'an explicitly.
B) Consensus (ijma') and its occurrence in some narrations.
C) It is a rational necessity.
D) The majority of linguists use it.

Key Takeaway
Using the name 'al-Qadim' for Allah is permissible, as established by consensus and supported by a hadith, despite some scholarly hesitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a matter where some scholars hesitate but clear evidence exists (like the name 'al-Qadim'), do you follow the evidence or remain in doubt? Examine your heart: is your hesitation rooted in piety or in a lack of trust in the consensus of scholars? What will you do to align your practice with the stronger proof?
?
The name 'al-Qadim' reminds you that Allah has always existed before anything else. In your daily struggles, do you truly live as if He is the Eternal One upon whom everything depends, or do you rely on temporary, created things? How can you concretely shift your reliance to Him this week?
?
The commentators debated the wording, but the ruling is clear. Are there areas of your religious practice where you avoid acting because you overthink minor differences? Will you commit to seeking the preponderant evidence and acting on it, even if it requires leaving behind comfortable hesitation?

Definition of Qidam (Eternity without Beginning)
Al-Bajuri defines qidam for God and creation, with terminological clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of qidam as it applies to God and to creation, and the terminological differences between theologians and linguists.

Core Idea
Qidam for God means absolute beginninglessness, not old age.

This lesson establishes the foundational attribute of qidam—eternity without beginning. Al-Bajuri first clarifies its meaning for God and contrasts it with its usage for created things, then explains how theologians and linguists differ in their technical terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage that defines qidam for God as 'absence of a beginning of existence' and for creation as 'length of duration.' Note the precise phrasing.
2
Understand the dual usage
Notice that qidam applies differently to God and to creatures. For God it is absolute; for creatures it is relative and fixed at one year in legal contexts.
The example of manumission illustrates the legal application.
3
Compare the two terminologies
The passage states that theologians take qidam literally for God and metaphorically for creation, while linguists reverse this. Reflect on why this difference matters.
This distinction affects how we interpret the term in theological discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقديم هو في حقه تعالى: عدم أولية الوجود، وإن شئت قلت: عدم افتتاح الوجود، وفي حق غيره - كما في قولهم: هذا بناء قديم -: طول المدة، وضبط بسنة، فإذا قال: كل من كان قديما من عبيدي فهو حر؛ عتق من له عنده سنة. وهو في اصطلاح المتكلمين حقيقة في الأول مجاز في الثاني، وفي اصطلاح اللغويين بالعكس. والصحيح أنه يجوز إطلاق القديم عليه تعالى؛ لثبوت ذلك بالإجماع، ووروده في بعض الروايات بدل: الأول. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري. (قوله: لثبوت ذلك بالإجماع أي بإجماع من يعتد بإجماعه، فالتأم مع ما قبله. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي. (قوله: لثبوت ذلك بالإجماع) أي: على وجه ينتج الجواز بحيث لا يكون الإطلاق على سبيل المشاكلة مثلا. وفيه: أنه إذا كان الإطلاق ثابتا بالإجماع؛ فلا معنى للخلاف في جواز الإطلاق المشار إليه بقوله: (والصحيح... إلخ)، ففي عبارته تناف وتناقض، لكن هو تابع في ذلك لعبارة الشرقاوي على الهدهدي. وعبارة المصنف في شرحه: وهل يجوز أن يتلفظ بلفظ القديم في حقه تعالى، فيقال: هو قديم؛ لأن معناه واجب له جل وعز عقلا ونقلا، أو لا يتلفظ بذلك، وإنما يقال: يجب له تعالى القدم ونحو هذا من العبارات، ولا يطلق عليه في اللفظ اسم القديم؛ لأن أسماءه جل وعز توقيفية؟ هذا مما تردد فيه بعض المشايخ، لكن قال العراقي في "شرح أصول الشبكي": عده الحليمي من الشافعية في الأسماء، وقال: لم يرد في الكتاب نصا، ولكن ورد في السنة، قال العراقي وأشار بذلك إلى ما رواه ابن ماجه في "سننه" من حديث أبي هريرة وفيه عد القديم في التسعة والتسعين. اهـ.
And al-qidam (eternity without beginning) is, in His right (exalted is He), the absence of a beginning of existence; and if you wish you may say: the absence of the inauguration of existence. And in the right of other than Him – as in their saying 'this is an ancient building' – it means length of duration, and it is fixed at one year; so if someone says: 'every one of my slaves who is qadim is free,' then the one who has been with him for a year becomes free. And it is, in the terminology of the theologians, literal in the first and metaphorical in the second, while in the terminology of the linguists it is the opposite. And the correct view is that it is permissible to apply the term al-qadim to Him (exalted is He), because that is established by consensus and because it occurs in some narrations instead of 'al-Awwal' (The First). Commentary of al-Allamah Ahmad al-Ajhuri. (His saying: 'because that is established by consensus') i.e. by the consensus of those whose consensus is considered, so ponder [?] along with what preceded. Annotations of al-Allamah Muhammad al-Anbabi. (His saying: 'because that is established by consensus') i.e. in a way that yields permissibility, so that the application is not, for example, as a rhetorical trope. And it is said: if the application is established by consensus, then there is no meaning to disagreement regarding the permissibility of the application indicated by his saying: (and the correct view... etc.), so in his phrase there is contradiction and inconsistency, but he is following in that the phrasing of al-Sharqawi on al-Hudhudi. And the wording of the author in his commentary: 'Is it permissible to utter the word 'al-qadim' concerning Him (exalted is He), saying 'He is Qadim', because its meaning is something due to Him (mighty and glorious) by reason and transmission, or should one not utter that, but rather say: 'al-qidam is due to Him, the Exalted' and similar expressions, and one does not apply to Him the name 'al-qadim' because His names (mighty and glorious) are tawqifiyya (revealed)? This is something about which some shaykhs have hesitated. But al-'Iraqi said in 'Sharh Usul al-Shaybki': al-Halimi from the Shafi'is counted it among the names, and said: it has not come in the Book textually, but it came in the Sunna. Al-'Iraqi said: and he indicated thereby the hadith narrated by Ibn Majah in his 'Sunan' from Abu Hurayra in which there is the counting of al-Qadim among the ninety-nine. End.'
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition and legal discussion. It begins by defining the attribute qidam (eternity without beginning) for God as the absence of a beginning of existence. It clarifies its usage for creatures, where it means long duration, technically defined as one year in manumission contexts. Then it distinguishes between the technical usage of theologians, who take it literally for God and metaphorically for creatures, and that of linguists, who reverse this. The correct view according to the author is that using the term 'qadim' for God is permissible, based on consensus and its occurrence in some hadiths as a synonym for 'al-Awwal'. The glosses then discuss potential contradictions in the author's phrasing, noting that if consensus established permissibility, there should be no disagreement. The discussion further presents the opinion of some scholars who hesitated due to the principle that divine names are tawqifiyya (only revealed through revelation), but al-'Iraqi reports that al-Halimi included it among the names, citing the hadith of Ibn Majah. The passage thus resolves the hesitation by showing that the name is indeed revealed.

Key Terms
القديم
القدم
أولية الوجود
المتكلمين
اللغويين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That qidam for God means 'old' in the same sense as an ancient building.
Correction: Qidam for God means absolute beginninglessness, not long duration; for creatures it means length of duration but is a different concept.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'qidam' mean when applied to God?
A) Length of duration
B) Absence of a beginning of existence
C) Being ancient in the sense of old
D) Having existed for a long time

Key Takeaway
Qidam for God is absolute beginninglessness; it is distinct from the relative 'oldness' of created things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Qadim,' do you truly sense that He has no beginning, or does your mind still imagine a starting point? How can you deepen your awareness of His eternal existence?
?
This lesson shows that the same word can mean different things for God and for creation. Are there other words you use for God (like 'merciful' or 'seeing') where you might be unconsciously thinking of a limited human version? What steps will you take to purify your understanding?
?
If God's qidam means He was never brought into existence, then your own existence is entirely dependent on Him. Does this truth affect how you live today? Will you commit to reflecting on your contingency at the start of each day?

Permissibility of Calling God Qadim
Al-Bajuri rules on the permissibility of using 'Qadim' for God, supported by consensus and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly debate on whether the term 'Qadim' can be applied to God and the evidence that establishes its permissibility.

Core Idea
The name 'Qadim' is permissible for God by consensus and hadith evidence.

After defining qidam, al-Bajuri addresses a practical question: may we actually call God 'Qadim'? Some scholars hesitated because divine names are tawqifiyya (only revealed). This lesson presents the arguments and the conclusion that it is allowed based on consensus and a hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the main ruling
Read the passage starting with 'And the correct view is that it is permissible to apply the term al-qadim to Him.' Note the two pieces of evidence given: consensus and narrations.
2
Examine the hesitation
The glosses mention that some shaykhs hesitated due to the principle that names of God are tawqifiyya. Understand this concern.
Tawqifiyya means names must be specifically revealed, not invented.
3
Review the counter-evidence
Al-'Iraqi reports that al-Halimi counted al-Qadim among the ninety-nine names based on a hadith from Ibn Majah. This resolves the hesitation.
