The Relationship Between Qadim and Azali
Al-Bajuri clarifies the relationship between qadim and azali, presenting two opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the terms qadim and azali, their relationship, and how they apply to both existent and non-existent attributes.

Core Idea
Eternal and ancient overlap, but not all scholars use them identically.

This passage from al-Bajuri's gloss investigates whether the terms al-qadim (the Eternal) and al-azali (the Beginningless) are synonymous or have different scopes. We will explore the verified conclusion and an alternative view, and then see how the term al-'adami (non-existent) is used for certain divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Let the author's own words establish the terms.
Notice that the passage begins with 'Wal-tahqīq...' (And the verified investigation...).
2
Identify the two views on qadim and azali
Look for the verified view (synonymous) and the alternative view (qadim specific to existent, azali more general). Understand their relationship of 'absolute generality and specificity'.
The alternative view is introduced with 'Qīla' (And it is said).
3
Understand the scope of 'adami (non-existent) attributes
Note how al-'adami is defined and what it includes: negative attributes and eternal relational matters. This clarifies the application of the terms.
The phrase 'kayāmu l-qudrati bi-dh-dhāti l-aqdasi' is an example of an eternal relational matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتحقيق: أن القديم والأزلي بمعنى واحد وهو ما لا أول له، وجودياً كان أو عدمياً. وقيل: القديم خاص بالوجودي، والأزلي أعم منه، وعليه يكون بينهما العموم والخصوص بإطلاق؛ لأنهما يجتمعان في الوجودي كذاته تعالى وقدرته وينفرد الأزلي في العدمي كالبقاء والمخالفة للحوادث. (قوله: أَوْ عَدَمِيّاً) المراد بالعدمي: ما قابل الوجودي، فيشمل العدمي حقيقة، وهي الصفات السلبية والأمور الاعتبارية القديمة كقيام القدرة بالذات الأقدس والأحوال القديمة على القول بثبوت الأحوال.
And the verified investigation is that al-qadim and al-azali (eternal) have one meaning: that which has no beginning, whether it be existent or non-existent. And it is said: al-qadim is specific to the existent, and al-azali is more general than it; and according to this, between them there is absolute generality and specificity, because they combine in the existent like His Essence (exalted is He) and His power, and al-azali alone applies to the non-existent like permanence (al-baqa') and difference from contingents (al-mukhalafa lil-hawadith). (His saying: 'or non-existent') the intended meaning of al-‘adami (non-existent) is that which is opposite to the existent, and it includes the truly non-existent – which are the negative attributes and the eternal relational matters like the subsistence of power with the most holy Essence, and the eternal states according to the view that states are established.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage, sourced from a gloss, presents an investigation into the relationship between two key theological terms: qadim and azali. The verified view holds that they are synonymous, both meaning 'that which has no beginning', applicable to both existential and non-existential entities. An alternative view restricts qadim to existential things, making azali broader. This results in a relationship of absolute generality and specificity: they overlap in existential matters (e.g., God's essence and power), but azali alone applies to non-existential attributes such as baqa' (perpetuity) and mukhalafa (difference from contingents). The passage then explains the term 'adami (non-existent) as covering negative attributes and eternal relational matters, citing examples like the subsistence of power in God's essence and the eternal states for those who affirm them. This careful distinction guards against confusion about the scope of these foundational attributes.

الأزلي
الوجودي
العدمي
العموم والخصوص
الصفات السلبية
الأمور الاعتبارية
الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that qadim and azali are entirely unrelated terms with completely different meanings.
Correction: The verified view is that they are synonymous, and even the alternative view holds that they overlap in many instances. They are not completely separate.

Checkpoint
According to the verified view in the passage, what does al-qadim mean?
A) That which has no beginning, whether existent or non-existent
B) Only existent things that have no beginning
C) Only God
D) That which lasts for a long time

Key Takeaway
The verified position is that qadim and azali are synonymous, meaning 'without beginning', applying to both existent and non-existent attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is eternal (azali),' do you truly grasp that He has no beginning—that before anything existed, He was, and His attributes were with Him? Does that reality make you tremble in awe, or do you just repeat the words? What will you change today to live with this awareness?
?
The attribute 'difference from contingents' (mukhalafa lil-hawadith) means Allah is unlike anything you can imagine. Think about your mental image of Allah. Is there any way you have unknowingly likened Him to creation? Ask Him to purify your belief and commit to learning His true attributes from revelation.
?
If both qadim and azali ultimately mean 'without beginning,' how does knowing that Allah's attributes are eternal affect your trust in Him? Can you recall a situation where you doubted His power or mercy? How should this lesson reshape your reliance on His unchanging attributes?

Definition of Baqa' and the Two Meanings of 'al-Akhir'
al-Bajuri defines baqa' and distinguishes two senses of 'akhiriyya'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine attribute of baqa' as the absence of an end, and the two distinct meanings of the term 'akhiriyya' and how they relate to the divine names al-Awwal and al-Akhir.

Core Idea
Allah's attribute of baqa' means He has no end, and 'al-Akhir' means He remains after all else perishes.

Al-Bajuri now explains the attribute of baqa' (eternal continuance). He gives a precise definition and then clarifies a potential ambiguity: the term 'akhiriyya' can refer either to extinction or to remaining after creation's annihilation. This distinction helps us understand the divine name al-Akhir correctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own definitions and distinctions come through.
Note that the passage is compact; each phrase carries weight.
2
Distinguish the two meanings of akhiriyya
Identify the two distinct senses: 1) extinction (al-inqida'), which is the opposite of beginning; 2) remaining after the annihilation of creation. Notice which sense the definition of baqa' uses and which gives the divine name.
The first sense is not applied to Allah's name.
3
Connect to the divine names
Understand that from the second sense of akhiriyya comes the name al-Akhir, and from the corresponding sense of awwaliyya (precedence) comes the name al-Awwal. This shows the names encompass both temporal and existential primacy/postcedence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْبَقَاءُ) هو في حقه تعالى: عدم آخرية الوجود، وإن شئت قلت: عدم اختتام الوجود. والآخرية تطلق على الانقضاء، وهو المراد هنا، ويقابلها بهذا المعنى: الأولية بمعنى الابتداء، وهو المراد فيما تقدم، وتطلق على: البقاء بعد فناء الخلق، ومنها بهذا المعنى اسمه تعالى الآخر، ويقابلها بهذا المعنى الأولية بمعنى السبق على الأشياء، ومنها بهذا المعنى: اسمه تعالى الأول.
(His saying: and al-baqa') is, in His right (exalted is He): the absence of the finality of existence; and if you wish you may say: the absence of the closure of existence. And al-akhiriyya (finality) is used to mean extinction, and that is what is intended here, and it is contrasted in this sense with al-awwaliyya (primacy) meaning beginning, which is what was intended previously. And it (al-akhiriyya) is also used to mean: remaining after the annihilation of creation, and from this latter sense is derived His name (exalted is He) al-Akhir (the Last), and it is contrasted in this sense with al-awwaliyya meaning precedence over things, and from this sense is derived His name (exalted is He) al-Awwal (the First).
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute baqa' (eternal continuance/perpetuity). For God, it means the absence of a terminal end to existence. The term 'akhiriyya' (finality) has two senses: firstly, extinction (al-inqida'), which is the sense meant here as the opposite of beginning; secondly, it means remaining after the annihilation of creation. From this second sense comes the divine name al-Akhir. The author also notes that the counterpart for the first sense of awwaliyya is 'beginning', while for the second sense it is 'precedence over things', from which comes the name al-Awwal. This clarifies that the names al-Awwal and al-Akhir encompass both temporal precedence/postcedence and existential primacy/remaining.

Key Terms
البقاء
الآخرية
الانقضاء
فناء الخلق
الآخر
الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Akhir only means 'the Last' in temporal order
Correction: Al-Akhir also means 'the Everlasting after creation ends'; it denotes remaining after annihilation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the divine name al-Akhir is derived from which sense of akhiriyya?
A) Extinction
B) Remaining after the annihilation of creation
C) Precedence over things
D) Beginning

Key Takeaway
The attribute of baqa' means Allah has no end, and 'al-Akhir' denotes His remaining after all creation has perished.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah's existence has no end, and everything in your life will end. What one attachment in your heart are you still holding onto as if it will never perish? Will you surrender it to the One who alone remains?
?
You just learned that 'al-Akhir' means Allah remains after the annihilation of all creation. Does your daily hope and fear reflect this truth? Who or what do you turn to for security—something that will end, or the One who never ends?
?
The attribute of baqa' means Allah has no closure. What does that imply for your relationship with Him? If you truly believed He will always be there, what would you dare to ask of Him today that you have been too afraid to ask?

Mukhalafa and the Four Negations
Al-Bajuri defines mukhalafa and enumerates four negations with brief definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's dissimilarity from contingent things (mukhalafa) logically negates four specific categories: jirmiyya, 'aradiyya, kulliyya, and juz'iyya.

Core Idea
God's difference from creation negates corporeality, accidents, composition, and divisibility.

Al-Bajuri begins by defining the divine attribute of mukhalafa as the absence of any likeness between God and His creation. From this single principle, he derives four essential negations that safeguard God's transcendence. This passage shows how a foundational theological concept yields concrete implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking, then read the English translation. Notice how the author first states the principle of mukhalafa and then lists four negations.
The passage is compact; each negation is given a brief definition.
2
Identify the four negations
Find and list the four terms: jirmiyya, 'aradiyya, kulliyya, juz'iyya. Understand what each one refers to based on the definitions provided.
Jirmiyya is about occupying space; 'aradiyya about accidents; kulliyya about being a whole; juz'iyya about being a part.
3
Connect each negation to mukhalafa
Reflect on why each negation follows necessarily from the idea that God is not like any contingent thing. Write a short statement for each.
For example, contingent things occupy space, so God does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَمُخَالَفَتُهُ تَعَالَى لِلْحَوَادِثِ) أي: عدم مماثلته تعالى لها، ويعلم من ذلك: نفي الجرمية والعرضية والكلية والجزئية. (قوله: الجرمية) هي التحيز المفسر بأخذ قدر من الفراغ. (والعرضية) هي قيام الصفة الحادثة بالذات. (قوله: والكلية) أي: ليس الله تعالى كلا لغيره بأن يكون غيره جزءا منه. (وقوله: والجزئية) معناه أنه تعالى ليس جزءا من غيره.
(His saying: and His dissimilarity (mukhalafatuhu), exalted is He, to the contingent things) means: the absence of His likeness to them, and from that is known the negation of corporeality (al-jirmiyya), accidentality (al-‘aradiyya), wholeness (al-kulliyya), and part-hood (al-juz’iyya). (His saying: al-jirmiyya) is occupying space (al-tahayyuz) interpreted as taking up an amount of vacant space. (And al-‘aradiyya) is the subsistence of an accidental attribute in a substance. (His saying: and al-kulliyya) means that God, exalted is He, is not a whole for another such that the other is a part of Him. (And His saying: and al-juz’iyya) means that He, exalted is He, is not a part of another.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of mukhalafa (difference from contingent things) as the absence of any likeness between God and His creation. From this negation of likeness, the author deduces four specific negations: (1) jirmiyya (corporeality) – God does not occupy space or take up volume; (2) ‘aradiyya (accidentality) – God does not have accidental attributes subsisting in a substance; (3) kulliyya (being a whole) – God is not composed of parts; (4) juz’iyya (being a part) – God is not a part of any larger whole. The glosses provide concise definitions: jirmiyya is defined as occupying space (tahayyuz) or taking up empty space, 'aradiyya as the subsistence of a created accident in a substance, kulliyya as not being a whole that has another as its part, and juz’iyya as not being a part of something else. These negations flow logically from the core idea that God is not like any contingent thing.

Definition of al-Baqa and the Two Meanings of Aakhiriyya
Al-Bajuri defines baqa for God and distinguishes two meanings of aakhiriyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of baqa (everlastingness) for God and the two distinct meanings of the term aakhiriyya (finality) in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Baqa is the absence of an end, not just endless duration.

In this passage, al-Bajuri defines the divine attribute of baqa (everlastingness) with precision. He begins by stating its meaning in relation to God, then clarifies that the term 'aakhiriyya' can be understood in two different ways. This distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting God's names al-Awwal and al-Aakhir.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a general sense of the definition and the distinction being made.
Pay attention to the terms 'aakhiriyya' and 'awwaliyya'.
2
Identify the two meanings of aakhiriyya
Notice that al-Bajuri gives two meanings: (1) expiration (inqida') and (2) remaining after the annihilation of creation. These correspond to two different uses of the word in theological contexts.
The first meaning is used in the definition of baqa; the second is related to God's name al-Aakhir.
3
Contrast each meaning with its opposite
For each meaning of aakhiriyya, al-Bajuri pairs it with a corresponding meaning of awwaliyya: (1) expiration vs. commencement, (2) remaining after creation vs. precedence over things.
This helps clarify the precise scope of each meaning.
4
Apply the distinction
Think about why this distinction matters: without it, one might misunderstand the attribute of baqa or the names al-Awwal and al-Aakhir.
The theological nuance ensures accurate discourse about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْبَقَاءُ) هو في حقه تعالى: عَدَمُ آخِرِيَّةِ الوجود، وإن شئتَ قلت: عدمُ اختتام الوجود. والآخِرِيَّةُ تُطْلَقُ على الانقضاء، وهو المراد هنا، ويقابلها بهذا المعنى: الأولية بمعنى الابتداء، وهو المراد فيما تقدم، وتطلق على: البَقَاءِ بعد فَنَاءِ الخلق، ومنها بهذا المعنى اسمه تعالى الآخِرُ ، ويقابلها بهذا المعنى الأولية بمعنى السَّبْق على الأشياء، ومنها بهذا المعنى: اسمُهُ تعالى الأَوَّلُ.
(His saying: 'and everlastingness (al-baqa)') It is, in respect of Him Exalted: the absence of finality (aakhiriyya) of existence. And if you wish, you may say: the absence of the termination of existence. And aakhiriyya is used to mean 'expiration' (al-inqida'), and that is what is intended here. Corresponding to it in this sense is awwaliyya (firstness) meaning 'commencement' (al-ibtida'), which is what was intended earlier. And [aakhiriyya] is also used to mean 'remaining after the annihilation of creation', and in this sense is His name al-Aakhir (the Last). Corresponding to it in this sense is awwaliyya meaning 'precedence over things', and in this sense is His name al-Awwal (the First).
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of al-baqa (everlastingness) for God. The commentator explains that in God's case, baqa means the absence of an end to existence—hence, God has no finality. He offers an alternative phrasing: the absence of termination. He then clarifies that the term aakhiriyya (finality) has two meanings. First, it means expiration or coming to an end; in this sense, it is the opposite of awwaliyya (firstness) meaning beginning. This is the meaning intended in the definition of baqa: God's existence has no expiration. Second, aakhiriyya can also mean remaining after the annihilation of creation, which is associated with God's name al-Aakhir. In this second sense, its opposite is awwaliyya meaning precedence over all things, associated with God's name al-Awwal. Thus, the commentator distinguishes the theological use from the everyday use.

Correction: The passage shows aakhiriyya has two meanings: expiration (end of existence) and remaining after creation. The first is used in defining baqa; the second relates to God's name al-Aakhir.

Four Negations from God's Incomparability
Al-Bajuri explains mukhalafah and lists four negations inferred from it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four specific negations that follow from the divine attribute of incomparability (mukhalafah) and their precise definitions.

Core Idea
God's incomparability with creation requires negating space, accident, whole, and part from Him.

Al-Bajuri unpacks the attribute of mukhalafah by showing that it implies more than just general dissimilarity. From the principle that God is unlike any created thing, he deduces four specific negations that safeguard divine transcendence. This passage defines these negations in precise terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise definitions given in parentheses.
2
Identify the four negations
The passage explicitly lists four things negated from God: jirmiyya, aradiyya, kulliyya, and juziyya. Locate each one and note how Al-Bajuri defines it.
These terms are technical; rely on the commentary for clarity.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
Consider why each negation follows from the attribute of incomparability. How do these negations protect the Islamic concept of tawhid?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَمُخَالَفَتُهُ تَعَالَى لِلْحَوَادِثِ) أي: عَدَمُ مماثلته تعالى لها، ويُعْلَمُ من ذلك: نَفْي الجِرْمِيَّة والعَرَضِيَّة والكُلِّيَّة والجُزْئِيَّة. (His saying: 'and His incomparability to originated things (al-hawadith)') Means: the absence of similarity between Him Exalted and them. From that is known the negation of corporeality (al-jirmiyya), accidentality (al-aradiyya), being a whole (al-kulliyya), and being a part (al-juziyya). (His saying: al-kulliyya) means that God is not a whole of which something else is a part. (His saying: al-juziyya) means that He is not a part of something else.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage explains the divine attribute of incomparability (mukhalafah li al-hawadith), which means God is not similar to any originated thing. From this negation of similarity, the commentator deduces four specific negations: (1) negation of jirmiyya (corporeality), meaning God does not occupy space; (2) negation of aradiyya (accidentality), meaning God does not have originated attributes inhering in a substance; (3) negation of kulliyya (wholeness), meaning God is not a composite whole with parts; (4) negation of juziyya (partness), meaning God is not a part of any larger whole. Each term is then defined: jirmiyya is defined as occupying a portion of space; aradiyya as the subsistence of a created accident in a substance; kulliyya is clarified to mean that God is not a whole that contains parts; and juziyya means God is not a part of anything else. This is a classic statement of the Ash'ari position on divine transcendence.

Objection: Why use 'hawadith' instead of 'mumkinat'?
Objection and answer about the scope of 'hawadith'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to using 'hawadith' to describe what God is incomparable to and how al-Ajhuri resolves it by distinguishing two interpretations.

Core Idea
The term 'hawadith' is defended by showing it can cover non-existents through careful interpretation.

This passage presents a subtle objection to Sanusi's choice of the word 'hawadith' (originated things) in his discussion of God's incomparability. The objector argues that 'hawadith' only covers existents, but God is also incomparable to non-existents. Al-Ajhuri's gloss provides a nuanced answer that clarifies the intended meaning. Let's explore how he resolves this concern while staying within the author's framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify why the objector thinks 'hawadith' is insufficient. Note the claim that it excludes non-existents.
The objection uses 'ma'dumat' (non-existents).
2
Understand the proposed alternative
The objector suggests 'mumkinat' (possible things) as a broader term. Ask yourself why that might be better.
3
Analyze al-Ajhuri's answer
Read how the gloss distinguishes two interpretations of God's incomparability to non-existents. Notice the conditional 'if they were to exist'.
The answer shows that the detailed incomparability applies conditionally.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how this answer justifies the author's use of 'hawadith' without needing to change to 'mumkinat'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الحوادثُ لا تَشْمَلُ المعدومات بل تختص بالموجودات، والمولى كما هو مخالف للموجودات مخالف للمعدومات، فهلا عبر المصنف بالممكنات الشاملة لكلّ من الموجودات والمعدومات؟ حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري :(قوله: مُخَالِفٌ لِلْمَعْدُومَاتِ إِن أريد بمخالفته لها عدم الموافقة من غير تفصيل إلى قولنا: (في الجرمية (والعرضية إلى آخر ما تقدم؛ كانت مخالفته لها ظاهرة، وإن أريد بمخالفته لها عدم موافقته لها على التفصيل إلى الجرمية وما بعدها؛ كان المراد بمخالفته للمعدومات أنها لو وجدت كان مخالفاً لها في الجرمية وما بعدها.
For it is said: Originated things (al-hawadith) do not include non-existents but are specific to existents, yet the Master, just as He is incomparable to existents, is also incomparable to non-existents. So why did the author not use [the term] possible things (al-mumkinat) which encompasses both existents and non-existents? Gloss by al-'Allamah Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'Incomparable to non-existents') If what is meant by His incomparability to them is non-correspondence without specification into jirmiyya, aradiyya, etc., then His incomparability to them is obvious. But if what is meant is non-correspondence to them in detail according to jirmiyya and what follows, then what is meant is that if they were to exist, He would be incomparable to them in jirmiyya and what follows.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection: the author (Sanusi) used the term 'hawadith' (originated things) to describe what God is incomparable to, but hawadith only refer to existing things, while God is also incomparable to non-existents (ma'dumat). So why not use the broader term 'mumkinat' (possible things), which includes both existents and non-existents? The answer, provided in a gloss by al-Ajhuri, distinguishes two possible interpretations of the phrase 'incomparable to non-existents'. If by that we mean a general non-correspondence without specifying the types of incomparability (like jirmiyya, aradiyya), then it is obvious that non-existents are not similar to God. But if we mean a detailed incomparability in terms of jirmiyya etc., then the meaning is that if non-existents were to exist, God would still be incomparable to them in those respects. The implication is that the author used 'hawadith' because the primary focus is on existent originated things, but the ruling can extend to non-existents by analogy or by interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hawadith only refers to existent things and cannot cover non-existents.
Correction: The gloss shows that when understood in detail, hawadith can be interpreted to cover non-existents as well, because God's incomparability to non-existents is understood conditionally.

Checkpoint

B) It does not include non-existents
C) It is not a Quranic term

Key Takeaway

The Meaning of 'Qa'im bi Nafsihi' and the Permissibility of 'Nafs' for Allah
Al-Bajuri explains the meaning of 'qa'im bi nafsihi' and argues for the permissibility of applying 'nafs' to Allah absolutely.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible grammatical interpretations of 'bi nafsihi' in describing Allah's self-subsistence, and the permissibility of applying the term 'nafs' to Allah absolutely based on Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Allah's self-subsistence is explained by two readings of 'bi nafsihi', and 'nafs' can be applied to Him directly, not merely metaphorically.

Al-Bajuri unpacks the phrase 'wa qiyamuhu bi nafsihi' from the creedal text, exploring its grammatical nuances to affirm Allah's absolute self-subsistence. He then defends the permissibility of using the term 'nafs' (self) for Allah without requiring metaphorical transference (mushakala), citing direct Quranic evidence. This lesson clarifies these foundational points in understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice how al-Bajuri moves from explaining the phrase to the broader ruling on 'nafs'.
The passage uses technical grammatical terminology.
2
Understand the two grammatical interpretations of 'bi'
Observe the two options: 'bi' as mulabasa (combination) or zarfiyya majaziyya (metaphorical circumstance). Both lead to the same meaning of subsistence being inherent to Allah's self.
This shows the precision of kalam in analyzing language.
3
Identify the evidence for applying 'nafs' to Allah
Find the Quranic verse (6:54) that directly applies 'nafs' to Allah, and note how al-Bajuri refutes those who restrict it to metaphorical usage. Also note the note on idafa (adding a thing to itself).
The passage contrasts with the verse about Jesus (5:116) as an example of mushakala.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَقِيَامُهُ بِنَفْسِهِ) أي: قياماً متلبساً بنفسه، فالباء للملابسة، ويحتمل أن تكون للظرفية المجازية. وعُلِمَ من كلام المصنف أنه يجوز إطلاق النفس عليه تعالى ولو من غير مشاكلةٍ، وهو كذلك، قال تعالى: (كَتَبَ رَبُّكُمْ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ الرَّحْمَةَ ) (1)، خلافاً لمن خَصَّهُ بالمشاكَلَة، كما في قوله تعالى حكاية عن عيسى تَعْلَمُ مَا فِي نَفْسِي وَلَا أَعْلَمُ مَا فِي نَفْسِكَ ) (2)، ودعواه أنها لا تُطْلَقُ إلا على ذي حياة عارضة؛ ممنوعة.
(٢)
وإضافةُ النفس للضمير في كلام المصنف ونحوه من قبيل إضافة الشيء لنفسه، فهما - وإن كانا شيئين من حيث العبارة - شيء واحد من حيث المعنى كما قاله الرَّاغِبُ (۳).
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: لِلظَّرْفِيَّة) هو وما قبله يرجعان إلى ارتباط القيام بذات الله تعالى، فهما معنيان
(قوله: مِنْ قَبِيلِ إِضَافَةِ الشَّيْءِ لِنَفْسِهِ غير ظاهر بل هي من إضافة العام للخاص
كشجر أراك.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي .
(قوله: وَدَعْوَاهُ) أي: من خَصَّهُ بالمشاكلة.
(His statement: 'and His being self-subsisting (wa qiyamuhu bi nafsihi)') means: a subsistence (qiyam) that is clothed (mutalabbis) with His self (nafsihi), so the preposition 'bi' is for combination (mulabasa), and it is possible that it is for metaphorical circumstance (zarfiyya majaziyya). And it is understood from the author's words that it is permissible to apply 'nafs' (self) to Him, exalted, even without association (mushakala), and it is so. Allah said: 'Your Lord has written mercy upon Himself (nafsihi)' (Quran 6:54), contrary to those who restrict it to association (mushakala), as in His statement quoting Jesus: 'You know what is in my self (nafsi) and I do not know what is in Your self (nafsika)' (Quran 5:116). And their claim that it is only applied to one possessing incidental life is rejected. (2) And the addition (idafa) of 'nafs' to the pronoun in the author's speech and similar cases is of the type of adding a thing to itself (idafat al-shay' li nafsihi), so they are, though two things in terms of expression, one thing in terms of meaning, as al-Raghib said (3). (Margin of the scholar Ahmad al-Ujhuri: His statement: 'for the circumstance (al-zarfiyya)' — it and what precedes it refer to the connection of subsistence (qiyam) with the essence of Allah, so they are two meanings. His statement: 'of the type of adding a thing to itself (idafat al-shay' li nafsihi)' is not apparent; rather it is an addition of the general to the specific, like 'shajar arak'. (Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: His statement: 'and his claim' — i.e., the one who restricted it to association (mushakala).
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary followed by critical glosses. The main text (al-Bajuri) explains the phrase 'wa qiyamuhu bi nafsihi' (and His being self-subsisting). Two possible grammatical readings of 'bi' are given: first, as the preposition of combination (mulabasa), meaning that the subsistence is inherent to His self; second, as metaphorical circumstance (zarfiyya majaziyya). Al-Bajuri then extracts a doctrinal point: from the author's usage, it is understood that it is permissible to apply the term 'nafs' (self) to Allah absolutely, without requiring the figure of 'mushakala' (association, i.e., using a word in a transferred sense because of a similarity). He provides Quranic evidence: 'Your Lord has written mercy upon Himself' (6:54). He refutes those who claim that 'nafs' can only be used for Allah in a metaphorical or associative sense, citing the verse where Jesus says 'You know what is in my self and I do not know what is in Your self' (5:116) as an example of mushakala. The claim that 'nafs' applies only to something with accidental life is denied. The text then notes an addition: the construction 'nafs' + pronoun (e.g., 'nafsihi') is an example of 'idafat al-shay' li nafsihi' (adding a thing to itself), where the two terms are expressively distinct but referentially identical, a point attributed to al-Raghib. Al-Ujhuri's gloss critiques this: he says the 'zarfiyya' interpretation and the previous one refer to the connection of subsistence with Allah's essence, so they are two meanings (indicating disagreement). He also objects to the classification as 'idafat al-shay' li nafsihi', arguing it is rather an addition of the general to the specific, like 'shajar arak' (a specific kind of tree). Finally, al-Anbabi's annotation clarifies that the 'claim' being rejected is that of the one who restricts the application of 'nafs' to Allah to mushakala.

Key Terms
قائم بنفسه
النفس
المشاكلة
الظرفية المجازية
الملابسة
إضافة الشيء لنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the term 'nafs' (self) can only be used for Allah in a metaphorical or associative sense (mushakala), and never directly.
Correction: The passage argues that 'nafs' can be applied to Allah absolutely, as shown by Quran 6:54 where Allah writes mercy upon His 'nafs' without any metaphorical transference. The verse about Jesus (5:116) is cited as an example of mushakala, but it does not restrict the application of 'nafs' to such contexts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the evidence that 'nafs' can be applied to Allah without association (mushakala)?
A) The verse 'Your Lord has written mercy upon His self'
B) The verse about Jesus saying 'You know what is in my self'
C) The opinion of al-Raghib
D) The claim that it is only for beings with accidental life

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'bi nafsihi' in describing Allah's subsistence can mean either combination or metaphorical circumstance, and applying 'nafs' to Allah is permissible absolutely, as shown by the Quran and contrary to restrictive views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah 'wrote mercy upon Himself' (Quran 6:54), do you truly believe His mercy is an unchangeable, essential attribute? How would your reliance on His mercy change if you saw it as a passing mood?
?
The passage refutes the idea that 'nafs' for Allah is just a metaphor. Have you ever treated any of Allah's attributes as mere symbols or figures of speech? What would it mean for your worship to take them as absolute realities?
?
Al-Bajuri explains that Allah's subsistence is completely independent, needing nothing. In what areas of your life do you act as if Allah needs your worship or obedience? How can you reorient your heart to worship out of need for Him, not out of a sense of obliging Him?

Understanding Qiyamuhu bi-Nafsihi and the Term Nafs for God
Al-Bajuri explains the phrase qiyamuhu bi-nafsihi and defends the unrestricted use of nafs for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the attribute 'qiyamuhu bi-nafsihi' (self-subsistence) and the evidence that the term 'nafs' (self) can be applied to God without metaphorical appropriation.

Core Idea
The term 'nafs' is used for God literally, proven by the Quran and rational analysis.

In this passage, Bajuri unpacks a key phrase from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin: 'qiyamuhu bi-nafsihi' (His being self-subsisting). He clarifies its linguistic meaning and then draws an important conclusion: that the word 'nafs' can be used for God directly, without needing a metaphor. He supports this with Quranic verses and addresses a counter-argument. This lesson will guide you through that explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overall sense of the discussion. Notice the structure: it starts with explaining 'qiyamuhu bi-nafsihi', then moves to the permissibility of 'nafs' for God, and ends with a grammatical note.
2
Understand the meaning of qiyamuhu bi-nafsihi
Focus on the opening explanation: 'qiyaman mutalabbisan bi-nafsihi' and the two possible interpretations of the ba (for adhesion or metaphorical container). Reflect on how this attribute emphasizes God's independence.
The ba is a key grammatical clue.
3
Examine the deduction about nafs
See how Bajuri infers that 'nafs' can be used for God without metaphorical appropriation (mushakala). He supports this with Quran 6:54 and refutes the opposing view that restricts it to metaphorical contexts.
The verse from Jesus (5:116) is used as an example of metaphorical use by others, but Bajuri argues the general principle.
4
Consider the grammatical point
Notice the discussion of 'idafat al-shay' li nafsih' (adding a thing to itself). Al-Bajuri states that in 'qiyamuhu bi-nafsihi', the word 'nafs' is added to the pronoun, making them conceptually one. Marginal notes offer alternative interpretations.
This is a subtle but important point in Arabic grammar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَقِيَامُهُ بِنَفْسِهِ) أي: قياماً متلبساً بنفسه، فالباء للملابسة، ويحتمل أن تكون للظرفية المجازية. وعُلِمَ من كلام المصنف أنه يجوز إطلاق النفس عليه تعالى ولو من غير مشاكلةٍ، وهو كذلك، قال تعالى: (كَتَبَ رَبُّكُمْ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ الرَّحْمَةَ )، خلافاً لمن خَصَّهُ بالمشاكَلَة، كما في قوله تعالى حكاية عن عيسى تَعْلَمُ مَا فِي نَفْسِي وَلَا أَعْلَمُ مَا فِي نَفْسِكَ، ودعواه أنها لا تُطْلَقُ إلا على ذي حياة عارضة؛ ممنوعة. وإضافةُ النفس للضمير في كلام المصنف ونحوه من قبيل إضافة الشيء لنفسه، فهما - وإن كانا شيئين من حيث العبارة - شيء واحد من حيث المعنى كما قاله الراغب. متقاربان حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: لِلظَّرْفِيَّة) هو وما قبله يرجعان إلى ارتباط القيام بذات الله تعالى، فهما معنيان (قوله: مِنْ قَبِيلِ إِضَافَةِ الشَّيْءِ لِنَفْسِهِ غير ظاهر بل هي من إضافة العام للخاص كشجر أراك. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي . (قوله: وَدَعْوَاهُ) أي: من خَصَّهُ بالمشاكلة.
(His words: 'and his being self-subsisting') means: a standing that is clothed in his self, so the ba is for adhesion, and it is possible that it is for metaphorical container. And it is understood from the author's speech that it is permissible to apply 'nafs' (self) to Him, exalted, even without metaphorical appropriateness, and it is so. He, exalted, said: 'Your Lord has prescribed mercy upon Himself' (Quran 6:54), contrary to those who restricted it to metaphorical appropriateness, as in His, exalted's, words related from Jesus: 'You know what is in myself and I do not know what is in yourself' (Quran 5:116), and his claim that it is only applied to one having accidental life is rejected. And the addition of 'nafs' to the pronoun in the author's speech and the like is of the type of adding a thing to itself, so they are two things in terms of expression but one thing in terms of meaning, as al-Raghib said. Close. Marginal note of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His words: 'for the container') it and what preceded it refer to the connection of self-subsistence to the essence of God, exalted, so they are two meanings. (His words: 'of the type of adding a thing to itself' is not apparent, rather it is an addition of the general to the specific, like 'shajaru arāk' (arak tree). Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His words: 'and his claim') meaning: the one who restricted it to metaphorical appropriateness.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the phrase قيامه بنفسه from Sanusi's matn. Al-Bajuri first explains that قياماً متلبساً بنفسه means a standing that is clothed in His own essence, with the ba serving for adhesion or metaphorical container. He then deduces from Sanusi's general statement that it is permissible to apply the term نَفْس to God without the need for rhetorical metaphor (مشاكلة), citing Quran 6:54 where God says He prescribed mercy upon His own 'self'. He refutes those who claim it is only used metaphorically (مشاكلة) by citing Quran 5:116 where Jesus uses it. Then he addresses the grammatical construction: adding نَفْس to a pronoun is like adding a thing to itself, meaning they are conceptually identical, as al-Raghib held. This is followed by marginal notes: al-Ajhuri comments that the two meanings (adhesion and container) both relate to the connection of qiyam to God's essence, and he objects to the 'addition of a thing to itself' interpretation, suggesting instead it is an addition of general to specific. Al-Anbabi clarifies that 'his claim' refers to the person who restricted the use to metaphor.

الباء للملابسة (al-bā' li al-mulābasa)
المشاكلة (al-mushākala)
إضافة الشيء لنفسه (idāfat al-shay' li nafsih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'nafs' (self) can only be used for God in a metaphorical sense (mushakala), never literally.
Correction: Al-Bajuri argues that the phrase 'qiyamuhu bi-nafsihi' and the Quranic evidence show that 'nafs' is applied to God literally, without needing metaphorical appropriation, as in Q. 6:54.

Checkpoint

A) The verse 'كتب ربكم على نفسه الرحمة' (Quran 6:54)
B) The verse 'تعلم ما في نفسي ولا أعلم ما في نفسك' (Quran 5:116)
C) The saying of al-Raghib
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The attribute 'qiyamuhu bi-nafsihi' affirms God's independence, and the term 'nafs' is applied to Him literally as evidenced by the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'Your Lord has prescribed mercy upon Himself,' do you feel the weight of that mercy as a real, eternal attribute of God, or do you secretly treat it as a metaphor because you cannot fathom His essence? How would your trust in Him change if you truly believed His mercy is as literal as His existence?
?
The author rejects the idea that 'nafs' only applies to temporary beings. Is there any part of you that unconsciously thinks God's attributes are somehow less 'real' or more abstract than human attributes? Why do you think that reservation exists, and what would it take for you to erase it?
?
Jesus said, 'You know what is in myself and I do not know what is in Yourself.' If you replaced 'Yourself' with 'my own soul,' would you tremble at the thought that Allah knows every secret of your being while you remain ignorant? How does this lesson push you to live with deeper awareness of His intimate knowledge?

Explanation of the Attribute لا يفتقر إلى محل and the Types of Wahid
Al-Bajuri explains the multiple meanings of two key attributes and clarifies their intended sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author explained these attributes, the multiple meanings of 'standing' and 'oneness', and the correct interpretation of God's independence from a place.

Core Idea
God does not need a place or a substratum, and His oneness is unique in kind.

Al-Bajuri begins by justifying why al-Sanusi explained the attributes 'does not need a place' and 'oneness' – because each term has multiple meanings that could lead to misunderstanding. He then clarifies that 'place' here means a substratum or essence, not a physical location, and that God's lack of need is derived from His being unlike creation. He also distinguishes three types of 'one' – individual, species, and genus – to show that God's oneness is not of any of these limited kinds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reason for explanation
Read the opening sentence where Al-Bajuri explains why the author clarified these attributes: because each term has multiple meanings that could confuse. Notice the warning that without explanation, one might misunderstand.
This sets the stage for the entire lesson.
2
Grasp the meanings of قيام and the true meaning of محّل
Identify the three meanings of قيام (standing, mastery, severity) and then see how Al-Bajuri defines محّل as an essence or substratum, not a place. Understand that God's lack of need for a place is derived from His being unlike contingent beings.
The al-Anbabi note clarifies that these meanings are not applicable when 'by Himself' is added.
3
Distinguish the types of واحد
Read al-Ajhuri's note about the three types of oneness: individual, species, and genus. Understand how each is defined and how God's oneness transcends all these categories.
This helps avoid thinking of God's oneness as a numerical or generic oneness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: أي: لَا يَفْتَقِرُ... إلخ) إنما فَسَّرَ المصنف هذه الصفة وما بعدها؛ لأن كلا منهما يُطْلَقُ على معانٍ ؛ إذ الأولى تُطْلَقُ على انتصاب القامة، وعلى: إحكام الشيء وإتقانه، يقال: قام فلان بكذا إذا أَحْكَمَه وأتقنه، وعلى: الشَّدَةِ، يقال: قَامَت الحرب على ساقها إذا اشْتَدَّ أمرُها. والثانيةُ تُطْلَقُ على وحْدَةِ الشخص ووحْدَةِ النوع ووحدة الجنس ونحوها من سائر الوحدات. (وقوله: إِلَى مَحَلَّ أي: ذات يقومُ بها، لا مكانٍ يَحِلُّ فيه؛ لأن عدم افتقاره تعالى إليه مأخوذ من مخالفته تعالى للحوادث. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وحْدَةِ الشَّخْصِ... إلخ) الواحد بالشخص ما تركب من مشخصات كاليدين والرجلين والطول المخصوص والعرض المخصوص وغير ذلك من المشخصات، أي: الأمور التي تميزه عن غيره، والواحد للنوع هو الأفراد المندرجة في نوع واحد، كأفراد بني آدم المندرجة في الإنسان، فتلك الأفراد يقال لها واحد بالنوع لاندراجها في نوع واحد، والواحد بالجنس هو الأنواع المندرجة في جنس واحد كالإنسان والفرس والحمار : - تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي. = (قوله: إذ الأُولَى تُطْلَقُ ... إلخ) فيه أن هذه المعاني لا تتوهم مع قوله: (بنفسه)، فالأولى أن يقال: إن المتوهّم هو قيامه بنفسه بمعنى: اسقلاله بأمور معايشه. (قوله: لِأَنَّ عَدَمَ افْتِقَارِهِ تَعَالَى إِلَيْهِ مَأْخُوذٌ مِنْ مُخَالَفَتِهِ... إلخ) أي: بخلاف عدم افتقاره إلى ذات يقوم بها، فإنه لا يُؤْخَذُ من المخالفة للحوادث لاحتمال أن يكون صفة قديمة قائمة بذات.
(His words: i.e., does not need... etc.) The author only explained this attribute and what follows because each of them is used for multiple meanings. For the first is used for upright stance, and for perfecting and mastering a thing; it is said: 'so-and-so stood by something' if he mastered and perfected it, and for severity; it is said: 'the war stood on its leg' if its matter intensified. And the second is used for oneness of the individual, oneness of the species, oneness of the genus, and similar onenesses. (His words: 'to a substratum' i.e., an essence that it subsists by, not a place it occupies; because the lack of need of Him, exalted, for it is derived from His being contrary to contingent beings. Marginal note by the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His words: 'oneness of the individual... etc.) The one by individual is what is composed of individualizing factors like hands, legs, specific length, specific width, and other individualizing factors, i.e., the matters that distinguish it from others. The one by species is the individuals subsumed under one species, like the individuals of mankind subsumed under 'human', so those individuals are called one by species due to their subsumption under one species. The one by genus is the species subsumed under one genus, like human, horse, and donkey. - Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: = (His words: 'since the first is used... etc.) It contains that these meanings are not imagined with his saying: 'by Himself', so it is better to say that what is imagined is His self-subsistence in the sense of His independence in the matters of His livelihood. (His words: 'because the lack of need of Him, exalted, for it is derived from His being contrary... etc.) i.e., unlike the lack of need for an essence it subsists by, for that is not derived from being contrary to contingents because of the possibility that it is an eternal attribute subsisting by the essence.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the attributes لَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى مَحَلٍّ (does not need a place) and صفة الوحدانية (oneness). Al-Bajuri begins by justifying why the author (Sanusi) explained these attributes: because each term has multiple meanings. The first word (قيام) can mean upright stance, mastery, or severity. The second (واحد) has types: individual oneness (وحدة الشخص), species oneness (وحدة النوع), and genus oneness (وحدة الجنس). Then he explains that 'to a substratum' means an essence that subsists, not a place; God's lack of need is derived from His being contrary to contingent things. Then al-Ajhuri's marginal note elaborates on the types of oneness: the one by individual is composed of distinguishing features; the one by species is the individuals under one type; the one by genus is the species under one general category. Then al-Anbabi's note offers an alternative interpretation: the various meanings of قيام are not imagined here because of the qualification 'by Himself', so the imagined meaning is God's independence. Finally, he clarifies that the lack of need for a place stems from God's being contrary to contingents, but the lack of need for an essence in which an attribute subsists is not derived from that, because an eternal attribute could be self-subsisting.

Key Terms
الافتقار إلى المحل (al-iftiqār ilā al-mahall)
وحدة الشخص (waḥdat al-shakhṣ)
وحدة النوع (waḥdat al-naw')
وحدة الجنس (waḥdat al-jins)
المشخصات (al-mushakhkhiṣāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'does not need a place' means God lacks a physical location, or that God's oneness is merely numerical or generic like created things.
Correction: The term 'place' here refers to any substratum or essence; God does not need any substratum. His oneness is not of the individual, species, or genus type; it is unique to His essence.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'one by species' (الواحد بالنوع)?
A) An individual composed of distinguishing features
B) Individuals subsumed under one type
C) Species subsumed under one genus
D) A single entity with no parts

Key Takeaway
God is absolutely independent of any need for a place or substratum, and His oneness is not like the oneness of individuals, species, or genera.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that God does not need a place or substratum. Have you ever caught yourself imagining God as being in a specific location—like above the sky or in the heavens? How does this lesson challenge that assumption? What will you do to purify your concept of God from such limitations starting today?
?
The lesson explains that God's oneness is not like the oneness of an individual, species, or genus. In your mind, have you ever compared God to something you know, even unconsciously? How can you train yourself to think of God's absolute uniqueness, and what concrete habit will help you avoid making comparisons?
?
Consider the statement 'God does not need any substratum.' This includes not needing any inner essence for His attributes to subsist. Does your soul rely on any created thing for its sense of security or identity? What do you need to let go of to truly depend only on God, the Self-Subsisting?

Three Types of Unity and Why Only One Fits Allah
Al-Ujhuri defines three kinds of unity and refutes their application to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of unity (personal, specific, generic) and why only the intended oneness applies to Allah.

Core Idea
Allah’s oneness is not like any created unity—it is beyond composition, species, or genus.

Before affirming that Allah is 'one', we must define what 'one' means. Al-Ujhuri clarifies three kinds of unity used for created things and shows that none of them can apply to the Creator. Understanding this distinction prevents gross misconceptions about tawḥīd.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the clear definitions and the logical conclusion.
2
Identify the three types of unity
Find and list the definitions for personal unity, specific unity, and generic unity. Pay attention to the examples (hands, feet, humans, animals).
Each type is defined by how many things it includes—one individual, one species, or one genus.
3
Understand why each type fails for Allah
For each type, note the reason it is impossible for Allah: personal unity implies composition, specific unity implies multiple individuals, generic unity implies multiple species.
Allah is free from all forms of multiplicity and composition.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Read the final sentence: all these meanings are invalid, and only the unity intended by the author (Sanusi) remains—that is, true oneness without any multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وحْدَةِ الشَّخْصِ... إلخ) الواحد بالشخص ما تركب من مشخصات كاليدين والرجلين والطول المخصوص والعرض المخصوص وغير ذلك من المشخصات، أي: الأمور التي تميزه عن غيره، والواحد للنوع هو الأفراد المندرجة في نوع واحد، كأفراد بني آدم المندرجة في الإنسان، فتلك الأفراد يقال لها واحد بالنوع لاندراجها في نوع واحد، والواحد بالجنس هو الأنواع المندرجة في جنس واحد كالإنسان والفرس والحمار والكلب المندرجة في الحيوان، فالواحد بالجنس هو تلك الأنواع لاندراجها في جنس واحد، فلو كان الله واحداً بالشخص؛ كان مركباً، ولو كان واحداً بالنوع؛ كان أفراداً متعددة، ولو كان واحداً بالجنس؛ كان أنواعاً متعددة، فبطلت إرادة تلك الوحدات كلها، وبقيت إرادة الوحدة بالمعنى الذي ذكره المصنف.
His saying: 'personal unity... etc.' The one by person (al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ) is that which is composed of individuators (mushakhkhiṣāt) such as the two hands, the two feet, particular height, particular width, and other such individuators, i.e. the matters that distinguish it from others. The one by species (al-wāḥid bi al-naw') is the individuals subsumed under one species, like the individuals of the children of Adam subsumed under 'human'. Those individuals are called 'one by species' due to their subsumption under one species. The one by genus (al-wāḥid bi al-jins) is the species subsumed under one genus, like human, horse, donkey, and dog subsumed under 'animal'. Thus the one by genus is those species due to their subsumption under one genus. If Allah were one by person, He would be composite; if He were one by species, He would be multiple individuals; if He were one by genus, He would be multiple species. Therefore intending all of those unities is invalidated, and intending the unity in the sense mentioned by the author remains.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a marginal note (hashiya) by al-Ujhuri explaining the three kinds of unity mentioned earlier. It defines each type clearly: (1) Personal unity (waḥdat al-shakhṣ) applies to an individual composed of distinguishing characteristics or individuators (mushakhkhiṣāt) like body parts and measurements. (2) Specific unity (waḥdat al-naw') refers to all individuals falling under one species, e.g., all humans considered as one species. (3) Generic unity (waḥdat al-jins) refers to multiple species falling under one genus, e.g., human, horse, dog under 'animal'. The author then argues that none of these can apply to Allah: personal unity implies composition (tarkīb), which is impossible for Allah; specific unity implies multiple individuals sharing the same species, which contradicts tawḥīd; generic unity implies multiple species under a genus, also impossible. Therefore, only the unity meant by al-Sanusi (i.e., indivisible oneness without any multiplicity) remains.

Key Terms
واحد بالشخص (wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ)
واحد بالنوع (wāḥid bi al-naw')
واحد بالجنس (wāḥid bi al-jins)
مشخصات (mushakhkhiṣāt)
تركيب (tarkīb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which kind of unity would require Allah to be composite?
A) Unity by species
B) Unity by genus
C) Unity by person
D) Unity by meaning

Key Takeaway
Allah’s oneness is incomposite, not a species or genus—it is a unique unity beyond all created categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes, even unconsciously, imagine Allah as a 'being' like a person with a 'form' or 'parts'? How does this lesson expose that subtle error in your tawheed?
?
If Allah were one by species or genus, He would be comparable to others. In your heart, do you ever subtly compare Allah to created things—even in mercy or power? How will you actively purify your conception?
?
The passage insists Allah’s oneness is absolutely unique. If you truly believed there is nothing like Him, how would your daily worship and reliance on Him change starting today?

The Objection Against Qidam as Proof of Self-Sufficiency
Al-Anbabi presents an objection to the claim that pre-eternity entails self-sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' definition of pre-eternity (qidam) as mere lack of beginning does not logically exclude the need for a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ).

Core Idea
Pre-eternity does not automatically mean no cause; cause and effect can both be eternal.

Al-Anbabi here addresses a subtle objection: if pre-eternity simply means 'no beginning,' then something could be eternal yet still require a cause—as long as that cause is also eternal. The philosophers used this reasoning to claim the celestial sphere is eternal yet created by God through causation. This lesson unpacks that objection and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The passage opens with 'قد يقال'... Read the Arabic and translation to understand the challenge.
The objection is against the claim that lack of need for a specifier is known from qidam.
2
Understand the definition of qidam
Note that the objectors define qidam as 'lack of beginning of existence' (عدم أولية الوجود).
This is a temporal definition, not an essential one.
3
Follow the logical possibility
They argue: from 'no beginning' it does not follow that there is no specifier, because the specifier could also lack a beginning.
Both the object and its specifier could be beginningless.
4
See the philosophers' example
The greatest sphere (al-falak al-a'ẓam) is pre-eternal yet has God as its specifier through causation (ta'līl).
For them, the effect of an eternal cause is itself eternal.
5
Grasp the distinction
Temporal pre-eternity (qidam zamānī) does not entail essential pre-eternity (qidam dhātī) for the philosophers.
This distinction is crucial for the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: لِأَنَّ عَدَمَ الْاِفْتِقَارِ إِلَى الْمُخَصِصِ مَعْلُومٌ مِنْ صِفَةِ الْقِدَمِ قد يقال: إنهم فشروا القِدَم بـ: عَدَمِ أوَّلية الوجود، ولا يلزم من كونه لا أؤل له أن لا يكون له مخصّص؛ لاحتمال أَوَّل أن يكون له مخصّص مع كونه ومخصصه لا أوَّلَ لهما، ولذلك قالت الفلاسفة: إن الفَلَكَ الأعظم ونحوه قديم، ومع ذلك له مخصّص وموجد، وهو الله تعالى، لكن بطريق التعليل؛ لأن معلول القديم قديمٌ فلا يلزم عندهم من القِدَمِ الزماني القدم الذاتي، وإن كان مذهب أهل السنة: أن كل قديم بالزمان قديم بالذات، إلا أنه في مقام ذِكْرِ الصفات ينبغي الاحتياط، فيصرّح بالصفات نظراً لعدم اللزوم عند الخصم، بل ذهب الأعاجم كالفَخْرِ والسَّعْدِ والعَضُدِ إلى أن صفاته قديمة بالزمان فقط، لأنها ناشئة عن المولى بطريق العِلةِ، فهي عندهم لذاتها واجبة لغيرها، لكن شَنْعَ ابن الظُّلُمُسَانِي على من قال بذلك كما في "الكبرى"، لكن البرهان الآتي في كلام المصنف لنفي المخصص لا يساعد هذا إلا بمعونة. فعليك بالتأمل.
His saying: 'because the lack of need for the specifier is known from the attribute of pre-eternity.' It may be said: They interpreted pre-eternity (qidam) as the lack of a beginning of existence. And from its having no beginning, it does not necessarily follow that it has no specifier, because it is possible from the first that it has a specifier while it and its specifier have no beginning. Therefore the philosophers said: The greatest sphere (al-falak al-a'ẓam) and the like are pre-eternal (qadīm), yet they have a specifier and a bringer-into-existence, namely Allah (may He be exalted), but by way of causation (ta'līl), because the effect of the pre-eternal is pre-eternal. Thus, according to them, temporal pre-eternity (al-qidam al-zamānī) does not necessitate essential pre-eternity (al-qidam al-dhātī). And although the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna is that everything pre-eternal in time is pre-eternal in essence, nevertheless, in the context of mentioning attributes, one should be cautious; one should explicitly mention the attributes because of the lack of logical necessity for the opponent. Rather, the non-Arabs (al-a'ājim) like al-Fakhr, al-Sa'd, and al-ʻAḍud went to the view that His attributes are pre-eternal only in time, because they arise from the Lord by way of causation; thus for them they are necessary by themselves for other than themselves. But Ibn al-Ẓulmusānī condemned those who said that, as in 'al-Kubrā'. However, the proof that follows in the author's speech for negating the specifier does not help this except with assistance. So upon you to reflect.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a marginal note (hashiya) by al-Anbabi that addresses an objection raised against the claim that 'lack of need for a specifier is known from the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam)'. The objection argues that qidam is defined as 'lack of beginning of existence' (adam awwaliyya al-wujūd), and from the lack of a beginning it does not necessarily follow that there is no specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ). It is logically possible for something to have a specifier while both the thing and its specifier are without beginning. The philosophers use this to argue that the greatest sphere (al-falak al-a'ẓam) and similar things are pre-eternal yet have a specifier, God, through causation (ta'līl), because the effect of a pre-eternal cause is itself pre-eternal. Thus for them, temporal pre-eternity does not entail essential pre-eternity. The note then contrasts this with the Sunni position that everything pre-eternal in time is also pre-eternal in essence. However, when discussing attributes, caution is needed because the opponent may not accept that entailment. The note mentions that some later scholars (al-Fakhr, al-Sa'd, al-ʻAḍud) held that divine attributes are pre-eternal only in time, arising from God by causation, making them necessary by themselves but for another. This view was condemned by Ibn al-Ẓulmusānī. The note concludes by stating that the upcoming proof in the author's text negating the specifier does not support the objection unless supplemented, advising the reader to reflect carefully.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
مخصص (mukhaṣṣiṣ)
أزل (azal)
تعليل (ta'līl)
قدم زماني (qidam zamānī)
قدم ذاتي (qidam dhātī)
فلاسفة (falāsifa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is pre-eternal, it must be self-sufficient and have no cause.
Correction: Pre-eternity as mere lack of temporal beginning does not rule out having an eternal cause; philosophers argue that an effect can be eternal if its cause is eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of qidam used by the objectors according to this passage?
A) Lack of need for a specifier
B) Lack of beginning of existence
C) Essential pre-eternity
D) Being necessary in itself

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' definition of pre-eternity as lack of beginning does not logically exclude the need for a specifier, because cause and effect could both be beginningless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas of your life—such as your health, your relationships, or your religious practices—that you treat as 'permanent' or 'self-sufficient' simply because they have been with you for a long time? How does this lesson challenge you to recognize their continuous dependence on Allah's specification and preservation?
?
The philosophers believed the celestial sphere was eternal yet caused. Do you ever assume that the regular patterns of nature (sunrise, seasons, etc.) are self-sustaining, forgetting that they are constantly brought into existence by Allah? What would it mean for you to live with that awareness every moment?
?
If temporal pre-eternity does not imply essential pre-eternity, then even the most ancient things in your life are not independent of Allah. What is one thing you have taken for granted as 'always there' that you now realize you must actively attribute to Allah's ongoing act of creation? How will you remind yourself of this tomorrow?

The Sunni Position and the View of the Non-Arabs on Divine Attributes
Al-Anbabi discusses the Sunni position and the alternative view of the non-Arabs regarding divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that temporal pre-eternity entails essential pre-eternity for attributes, and the contrasting view of some later scholars.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Sunna holds that whatever is eternal in time is also eternal in essence, but opponents disagree.

After presenting the philosophers' objection, al-Anbabi now contrasts the Sunni doctrine with the views of certain later theologians (al-a'ājim) who maintained that God's attributes are only temporally pre-eternal, arising through causation. This reveals a nuanced debate about the nature of divine attributes and their eternity.

Guided Path
1
State the Sunni position
The text says 'وإن كان مذهب أهل السنة: أن كل قديم بالزمان قديم بالذات'.
This is the orthodox view.
2
Note the caution needed
'إلا أنه في مقام ذكر الصفات ينبغي الاحتياط' - one must be careful in mentioning attributes, because the opponent may not accept the entailment.
Use explicit language to avoid confusion.
3
Identify the non-Arabs' view
'ذهب الأعاجم كالفخر والسعد والعضد إلى أن صفاته قديمة بالزمان فقط'
These are noted scholars: al-Rāzī, al-Taftāzānī, al-Ījī.
4
Understand their reasoning
Attributes arise from God by way of causation (علة), so they are necessary by themselves for other than themselves.
They are wājib bi al-ghayr.
5
Note the condemnation
Ibn al-Ẓulmusānī condemned this view, as mentioned in 'al-Kubrā'.
This shows the controversy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Concluding Advice: Upcoming Proof Needs Supplementation
Al-Anbabi warns that the author's proof against the specifier is not sufficient without further aid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the author's upcoming proof negating the specifier does not automatically resolve the objection without further deliberation.

Core Idea
The proof to come needs supplementation; reflection is required.

Al-Anbabi closes his note with a crucial warning. The proof that the author (al-Sanūsī) will present later in the text to negate the need for a specifier does not, on its own, refute the objection just discussed. It requires additional support. This lesson highlights the need for careful reflection (ta'ammul).

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding remark
Read the final two sentences: 'لكن البرهان الآتي... فعليك بالتأمل'.
This is the end of the marginal note.
2
Understand the limitation
The upcoming proof does not help the objection without assistance (بمعونة).
It is not self-sufficient.
3
Reflect on the implication
The objection remains a challenge that needs a more nuanced answer. Al-Anbabi advises deep reflection.
This underscores the complexity of the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: لِأَنَّ عَدَمَ الْاِفْتِقَارِ إِلَى الْمُخَصِصِ مَعْلُومٌ مِنْ صِفَةِ الْقِدَمِ قد يقال: إنهم فشروا القِدَم بـ: عَدَمِ أوَّلية الوجود، ولا يلزم من كونه لا أؤل له أن لا يكون له مخصّص؛ لاحتمال أَوَّل أن يكون له مخصّص مع كونه ومخصصه لا أوَّلَ لهما، ولذلك قالت الفلاسفة: إن الفَلَكَ الأعظم ونحوه قديم، ومع ذلك له مخصّص وموجد، وهو الله تعالى، لكن بطريق التعليل؛ لأن معلول القديم قديمٌ فلا يلزم عندهم من القِدَمِ الزماني القدم الذاتي، وإن كان مذهب أهل السنة: أن كل قديم بالزمان قديم بالذات، إلا أنه في مقام ذِكْرِ الصفات ينبغي الاحتياط، فيصرّح بالصفات نظراً لعدم اللزوم عند الخصم، بل ذهب الأعاجم كالفَخْرِ والسَّعْدِ والعَضُدِ إلى أن صفاته قديمة بالزمان فقط، لأنها ناشئة عن المولى بطريق العِلةِ، فهي عندهم لذاتها واجبة لغيرها، لكن شَنْعَ ابن الظُّلُمُسَانِي على من قال بذلك كما في "الكبرى"، لكن البرهان الآتي في كلام المصنف لنفي المخصص لا يساعد هذا إلا بمعونة. فعليك بالتأمل.
His saying: 'because the lack of need for the specifier is known from the attribute of pre-eternity.' It may be said: They interpreted pre-eternity (qidam) as the lack of a beginning of existence. And from its having no beginning, it does not necessarily follow that it has no specifier, because it is possible from the first that it has a specifier while it and its specifier have no beginning. Therefore the philosophers said: The greatest sphere (al-falak al-a'ẓam) and the like are pre-eternal (qadīm), yet they have a specifier and a bringer-into-existence, namely Allah (may He be exalted), but by way of causation (ta'līl), because the effect of the pre-eternal is pre-eternal. Thus, according to them, temporal pre-eternity (al-qidam al-zamānī) does not necessitate essential pre-eternity (al-qidam al-dhātī). And although the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna is that everything pre-eternal in time is pre-eternal in essence, nevertheless, in the context of mentioning attributes, one should be cautious; one should explicitly mention the attributes because of the lack of logical necessity for the opponent. Rather, the non-Arabs (al-a'ājim) like al-Fakhr, al-Sa'd, and al-ʻAḍud went to the view that His attributes are pre-eternal only in time, because they arise from the Lord by way of causation; thus for them they are necessary by themselves for other than themselves. But Ibn al-Ẓulmusānī condemned those who said that, as in 'al-Kubrā'. However, the proof that follows in the author's speech for negating the specifier does not help this except with assistance. So upon you to reflect.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a marginal note (hashiya) by al-Anbabi that addresses an objection raised against the claim that 'lack of need for a specifier is known from the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam)'. The objection argues that qidam is defined as 'lack of beginning of existence' (adam awwaliyya al-wujūd), and from the lack of a beginning it does not necessarily follow that there is no specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ). It is logically possible for something to have a specifier while both the thing and its specifier are without beginning. The philosophers use this to argue that the greatest sphere (al-falak al-a'ẓam) and similar things are pre-eternal yet have a specifier, God, through causation (ta'līl), because the effect of a pre-eternal cause is itself pre-eternal. Thus for them, temporal pre-eternity does not entail essential pre-eternity. The note then contrasts this with the Sunni position that everything pre-eternal in time is also pre-eternal in essence. However, when discussing attributes, caution is needed because the opponent may not accept that entailment. The note mentions that some later scholars (al-Fakhr, al-Sa'd, al-ʻAḍud) held that divine attributes are pre-eternal only in time, arising from God by causation, making them necessary by themselves but for another. This view was condemned by Ibn al-Ẓulmusānī. The note concludes by stating that the upcoming proof in the author's text negating the specifier does not support the objection unless supplemented, advising the reader to reflect carefully.

Key Terms
برهان (burhān)
مخصص (mukhaṣṣiṣ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's upcoming proof will fully refute the objection without need for further thought.
Correction: Al-Anbabi says the proof does not help except with assistance, so the objection still requires careful reflection.

Checkpoint
According to al-Anbabi, what does the upcoming proof in the author's speech not do?
A) It fully negates the specifier
B) It helps the objection without assistance
C) It is irrelevant
D) It confuses the issue

Key Takeaway
The author's proof against the specifier is insufficient on its own and requires additional support to counter the philosophical objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological argument that seems clear, do you ever stop to consider whether it might need 'assistance' — hidden premises or further reasoning — before it truly convinces? How does Al-Anbabi's warning change the way you should read the next proof in this book?
?
What role does deep reflection (تأمل) play in your own learning of aqeedah? Have you been treating proofs as self-contained facts, or are you willing to sit with them, identify their weaknesses, and seek additional understanding? Take a moment to commit to one practical change in how you study.

Objection to Qidam Implying No Specifier
Commentator addresses objection that qidam alone does not imply lack of need for a specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why qidam (pre-eternity) alone does not logically entail the absence of a specifier, and how the Sunni position differs from the philosophers' view.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity does not automatically rule out a cause; temporal and essential pre-eternity are distinct.

The commentator addresses a subtle objection: if qidam is merely the negation of a beginning, it doesn't follow that something pre-eternal has no cause. The philosophers use this to argue that the universe can be pre-eternal yet created by God. In this passage, we see how careful theological reasoning must distinguish types of pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Focus on the logical flow of the objection and response.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection
Find the statement that questions whether qidam implies no need for a specifier. Note the definition of qidam used.
The objection hinges on the possibility of both the thing and its specifier being beginningless.
3
Understand the philosophers' argument
See how the philosophers use this to claim the celestial spheres are pre-eternal yet have a cause. Note their distinction between temporal and essential pre-eternity.
4
Reflect on the Sunni position and caution
Consider the Sunni view that temporal pre-eternity entails essential pre-eternity, but why caution is needed in debate. Note the mention of later theologians and the author's proof.
The passage ends with an invitation to reflect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: لِأَنَّ عَدَمَ الْاِفْتِقَارِ إِلَى الْمُخَصِصِ مَعْلُومٌ مِنْ صِفَةِ الْقِدَمِ قد يقال: إنهم فسروا القِدَم بـ: عَدَمِ أوَّلية الوجود، ولا يلزم من كونه لا أؤل له أن لا يكون له مخصّص؛ لاحتمال أَوَّل أن يكون له مخصّص مع كونه ومخصصه لا أوَّلَ لهما، ولذلك قالت الفلاسفة: إن الفَلَكَ الأعظم ونحوه قديم، ومع ذلك له مخصّص وموجد، وهو الله تعالى، لكن بطريق التعليل؛ لأن معلول القديم قديمٌ فلا يلزم عندهم من القِدَمِ الزماني القدم الذاتي، وإن كان مذهب أهل السنة: أن كل قديم بالزمان قديم بالذات، إلا أنه في مقام ذِكْرِ الصفات ينبغي الاحتياط، فيصرّح بالصفات نظراً لعدم اللزوم عند الخصم، بل ذهب الأعاجم كالفَخْرِ والسَّعْدِ والعَضُدِ إلى أن صفاته قديمة بالزمان فقط، لأنها ناشئة عن المولى بطريق العِلةِ، فهي عندهم لذاتها واجبة لغيرها، لكن شَنَّعَ ابن الظُّلُمُسَانِي على من قال بذلك كما في "الكبرى"، لكن البرهان الآتي في كلام المصنف لنفي المخصص لا يساعد هذا إلا بمعونة. فعليك بالتأمل.
His statement: 'Because the lack of need for a specifier is known from the attribute of qidam (pre-eternity)' – it may be said: They defined qidam as non-existence of priority of existence, and it does not necessarily follow from its having no beginning that it should have no specifier; for it is possible that it has a specifier while both it and its specifier have no beginning. Therefore, the philosophers said: the greatest sphere and the like are pre-eternal, yet they have a specifier and an existentiator, which is God Most High, but by way of causation; because the effect of a pre-eternal thing is pre-eternal, so according to them, temporal pre-eternity does not entail essential pre-eternity. Even though the school of Ahl al-Sunna is that everything temporally pre-eternal is essentially pre-eternal, nevertheless, when mentioning the attributes, caution should be exercised, so one should specify the attributes due to the lack of entailment from the opponent's perspective. Rather, the non-Arabs such as al-Fakhr [al-Razi], al-Sa'd [al-Taftazani], and al-'Adud [al-Iji] held that His attributes are pre-eternal only temporally, because they arise from the Lord by way of causation; so according to them, they are necessary in themselves for the sake of another. However, Ibn al-Zulumsani condemned those who said that, as in 'al-Kubra'. But the proof forthcoming in the author's speech for denying the specifier does not support this except with assistance. So reflect.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection to the claim that lack of need for a specifier is known from the attribute of qidam (pre-eternity). The objector argues that qidam is defined as 'the negation of priority of existence,' and it does not follow from being beginningless that it has no specifier, because it is possible that both the thing and its specifier are beginningless. The philosophers use this to argue that the celestial spheres are pre-eternal yet have God as their specifier and cause, as the effect of a pre-eternal cause is also pre-eternal. According to the philosophers, temporal pre-eternity (qidam zamani) does not entail essential pre-eternity (qidam dhati). However, the Sunni position is that everything temporally pre-eternal is essentially pre-eternal, but even so, when discussing divine attributes, one should be cautious and explicitly state the attributes to avoid the opponent's objection. The commentator also notes that some later theologians like al-Razi, al-Taftazani, and al-Iji held that God's attributes are only temporally pre-eternal, being effects of God's essence by way of causation. This view was condemned by Ibn al-Zulumsani. The proof that will be given by the author (Sanusi) to negate a specifier does not support this position except with assistance. The passage ends with an exhortation to reflect.

مخصص
