Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is pre-eternal (qadim), it must have no cause or specifier.
Correction: Qidam defined as beginninglessness does not rule out a specifier, because both the thing and its specifier could be beginningless. The philosophers accept temporal pre-eternity but deny essential pre-eternity for created things.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers argue that the celestial spheres can be pre-eternal yet have a specifier?
A) Because they define qidam as essential necessity
B) Because the effect of a pre-eternal cause is pre-eternal, so temporal pre-eternity does not preclude a cause
C) Because they deny the existence of God
D) Because they believe in eternal creation

Key Takeaway
Qidam as mere beginninglessness does not rule out a specifier; only Allah is essentially pre-eternal, and caution is needed in theological debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate Allah's attribute of qidam, do you truly grasp that He is not just temporally before creation, but essentially self-sufficient and necessary? How does this lesson challenge any subtle assumption you might have that Allah's eternity is merely a matter of time?
?
If someone argued that because the universe is beginningless (as some philosophers claim), it does not need a creator, how would you use the distinction between temporal and essential pre-eternity to refute them? Are you convinced enough to defend this with confidence?
?
The lesson warns that theological debates require caution and precision. In your own life, have you ever oversimplified a matter of faith to avoid complexity? Are you willing to engage with difficult distinctions for the sake of deeper truth, even when it is uncomfortable?

The Propriety of Language Regarding Allah's Attributes: Critique of al-Razi's Use of 'Iftiqar'
Commentator rebukes Fakhr al-Razi for using the term iftiqar (need) for God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'iftiqar' is considered inappropriate for describing the relationship between Allah's attributes and His essence, and the importance of proper theological language.

Core Idea
Words matter: even a seemingly accurate description can be disrespectful if it implies deficiency in Allah.

In this brief but significant passage, the commentator takes Fakhr al-Din al-Razi to task for his choice of words when discussing the divine attributes. While al-Razi's underlying reasoning was correct—the attributes cannot subsist independently—the term 'iftiqar' (need) carries unseemly connotations. This lesson examines why theological precision and respect (adab) must go hand in hand.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Fakhr al-Din al-Razi used the term iftiqar to describe how the attributes subsist in Allah's essence. Read the Arabic carefully to see the commentator's reaction.
The term iftiqar is usually rendered as 'need' or 'dependency'.
2
Identify the criticism
The commentator says al-Razi 'asā'a al-adab' (lacked good manners). Find the reasons given: al-Razi's perspective and his heedlessness.
3
Consider the implication
Why is iftiqar problematic? It suggests that the attributes are deficient without the essence, whereas in reality they are perfect and necessarily subsistent.
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how the choice of words in theology can affect the understanding of Allah's perfection. This teaches us to be cautious in our own speech about Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أساءَ الفَخْرُ الأدب حيث عَبَّرَ في هذا القسم بالافتقار، نظراً منه إلى استحالة قيام صفاته تعالى بنفسها ووجوب قيامه بالذات الأقدس، مع غفلته عما يُوهِمُ التعبير بالافتقار.
And al-Fakhr [al-Razi] lacked good manners (asā'a al-adab) when he expressed in this section with iftiqar (need), from his perspective of the impossibility of the attributes of the Most High subsisting by themselves and the necessity of their subsisting in the most holy essence, while being heedless of what the expression 'iftiqar' implies.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This brief but sharp passage criticizes Fakhr al-Din al-Razi for using the term 'iftiqar' (need, dependency) to describe the relationship of God's attributes to His essence. The commentator argues that this is a breach of proper respect (asā'a al-adab). Fakhr al-Razi's reasoning was that since the attributes cannot subsist by themselves, they must subsist in the essence, and he called that 'need' for the essence. However, the commentator points out that the term 'iftiqar' carries a connotation of deficiency and lack, which is inappropriate for the divine attributes. The attributes are not 'needy' of the essence in a way that implies poverty; rather, they are necessarily subsistent in the essence. The criticism is that Fakhr al-Din failed to consider the misleading implication of his wording.

Key Terms
افتقار
الأدب
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That describing attributes as 'in need of the essence' is acceptable as long as the underlying logic is correct.
Correction: The term 'need' implies deficiency, which cannot be attributed to Allah or His attributes. Proper language must avoid any hint of imperfection, even in analogical reasoning.

Checkpoint
Why did the commentator criticize Fakhr al-Din al-Razi?
A) He denied the existence of attributes
B) He used the term iftiqar which implies deficiency
C) He claimed attributes are accidents
D) He believed attributes are separate from God

Key Takeaway
Theological language must be not only accurate but also reverent; terms that imply deficiency, even indirectly, are to be avoided when speaking of Allah's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah in your daily life or in worship, do you ever use words that are technically correct but lack the depth of reverence He deserves? What phrase might you need to change, and how will you consciously replace it this week?
?
This lesson shows that even a scholar like al-Razi can slip into language that implies deficiency about Allah. What does that reveal about your own need for constant vigilance in how you think and speak about your Creator? Will you ask someone to gently correct you if they hear you using casual or inappropriate terms?
?
If someone described you with a factually accurate word that carried a demeaning connotation, you would feel hurt. Why, then, do we sometimes settle for 'just accurate' when describing the One who deserves the most exalted language? Commit to memorizing a reverent phrase for each of Allah's attributes you learn.

Why al-Razi's 'Iftiqar' Is Problematic
Critique of al-Razi's term 'iftiqar' for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Fakhr al-Razi's use of 'iftiqar' (neediness) for divine attributes is criticized for suggesting deficiency in God.

Core Idea
Language about God must avoid any implication of need or lack.

Theologians are careful with every word used about Allah. In this passage, the author critiques Fakhr al-Razi for using the term 'iftiqar' (neediness) to describe how divine attributes relate to the essence. At first glance, it might seem technical, but the critique reveals why this word is inappropriate: it suggests a deficiency that can never apply to Allah. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage from al-Bajuri's Hashiyah. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the word 'iftiqar' and how it is being criticized.
The passage is short but dense—take your time.
2
Understand the critique
The author says al-Razi was 'discourteous' because his wording implies something negative about Allah. Ask: what is wrong with saying attributes are 'in need' of the essence?
Consider the connotation of 'need' in everyday language.
3
Connect to the core idea
The lesson is that theological terms must be free of any hint of imperfection. The commentary clarifies that 'need' suggests a lack—something Allah is free from.
This is a key principle in Islamic theology (kalam).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أساءَ الفَخْرُ) الأدب حيث عَبَّرَ في هذا القسم بالافتقار، نظراً منه إلى استحالة قيام صفاته تعالى بنفسها ووجوب قيامه بالذات الأقدس، مع غفلته عما يُوهِمُ التعبير بالافتقار. (قوله: (عَمَّا يُوهِمُهُ التَّعْبِيرُ بِالْاِفْتِقَارِ) وهو فقد شيء يحتاج إليه المفتقر، فإن قولك: فلان محتاج إلى الأكل يوهم أنه فاقد للطعام.
And al-Fakhr [al-Razi] was discourteous when he expressed in this section with 'neediness' (iftiqar), considering from his perspective the impossibility of His attributes subsisting by themselves and the necessity of their subsistence by the most holy essence, while he was heedless of what the expression with 'neediness' suggests. (His saying: concerning what the expression with neediness suggests) — it is the loss of something which the needy one requires, for your saying: 'So-and-so needs food' suggests that he is lacking food.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents a critique of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's use of the term 'iftiqar' (neediness) in describing the relationship between God's essence and His attributes. The author (likely al-Bajuri) argues that al-Razi was discourteous (asa'a al-adab) because the term implies deficiency or lack, which is inappropriate for God. Al-Razi used it based on the idea that attributes cannot subsist independently and must be attached to the essence, but the author points out that 'iftiqar' suggests the opposite—that the attributes are lacking something. The commentary by al-Ajhuri then clarifies that saying someone is 'in need' (muhtaj) of something, like food, implies they are lacking that item, and applying such terminology to God's attributes is problematic.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'iftiqar' is a neutral theological term without negative implications.
Correction: This passage shows that 'iftiqar' implies need and deficiency, which cannot be attributed to God, so the term is inappropriate.

Checkpoint

B) Because it suggests God is lacking something
C) Because it is a bid'ah term
D) Because al-Fakhr was a philosopher

Key Takeaway
Every word used to describe Allah must be free of any implication of need or deficiency; 'iftiqar' fails this test.

Oneness of Attributes: Negating Multiplicity and Similitude
Sanusi defines oneness of attributes via two negations and addresses a difficulty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of oneness of attributes as the negation of multiple attributes of the same genus and the negation of any attribute in creation exactly like God's.

Core Idea
God's attributes are unique: no two of the same kind, and none exactly like His.

Having established God's oneness in essence, Sanusi now turns to the oneness of His attributes. This means not only that God does not have multiple powers or wills, but also that no creature can possess an attribute that is identical to God's attribute in perfection. This lesson explains these two negations and addresses a subtle objection about the impossibility of 'continuous quantity' in attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the definition sink in before interpreting.
Notice the two parts: negating multiplicity of same genus, and negating an attribute like God's in another.
2
Identify the two negations
The passage defines oneness of attributes as two negations: (1) no multiple attributes of the same genus (e.g., two powers), (2) no attribute in another being that is exactly like God's attribute. Find these in the text.
The second negation is explained: a similar but not identical attribute does not harm.
3
Understand the difficulty and its answer
The text raises a difficulty: how can 'continuous quantity' (multiple attributes of same genus) be conceived when it requires physical composition? Read the answer: the subsistence of multiple attributes in one essence is treated as having the status of composition, not actual composition.
This answer uses an analogy to resolve the conceptual problem.
4
Study the gloss on metaphorical 'quantity'
Al-Ajhuri explains that applying 'quantity' to the existence of a similar attribute is metaphorical—quantity is the number that would arise if such an attribute existed. Also note his clarification of 'status of composition' as referring to composition from individual atoms, not attributes.
This helps avoid misunderstanding the terminology.
5
Reflect on the main takeaway
Summarize in your own words: Oneness of attributes means God has no duplicate attributes and no creature has an attribute identical to His. Why is this important for tawhid?
It preserves God's uniqueness and perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووحدانية في الصفات ومعناها: عدم تعدُّدِ الصفات للذات الأقدس من جنيس واحدٍ، كأن يكون له قُدْرَتان فأكثر، أو إرادتان فأكثر، أو علمان فأكثر، خلافاً لمن قال بتعددِ ذلك بتعدد المتعلقات، وعدم ثبوتِ صِفَةٍ لغيره كصفته تعالى، كأن يكون لغيره قدرةً كقدرته تعالى، وأما أن يكون لغيره قدرة لا كقدرته تعالى؛ فلا تعدد الصفات للذات يَضُرُّ، فهي عبارة عن نفي الكم المتصل في الصفات وهو المقدسة من جنس واحدٍ كما تقدم، وعن نفي الكم المنفصل في الصفات: وهو ثبوتُ صِفَةٍ لغيره كصفته تعالى كما تقدم أيضاً. ويُحِثَ في تصوير الكم المتصل في الصفات؛ لأنه لا بُدَّ فيه من الاتصال والتركب من أجزاء، وهو مُنتَف هنا وأجيب بأن قيام الصفات من جنيس واحدٍ بالذات الواحدة مُنَزَّل منزلة التركب. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وَهُوَ ثُبُوتُ صِفَةٍ) إطلاق الكم على هذا الثبوت من باب إطلاق اسم المسبب على السبب؛ لأن الكم حقيقةً هو العدد القائم بصفة الله وصفة غيره المشابهة لصفته على تقدير وجودها، وبانتفاء تلك الصفة ينتفي الكم. (قوله: مَنْزِلَةَ التَّرَكُب) أي: من جواهر فردة لا من صفات؛ لأن الكم المنفصل من عوارض الجسم، وهو ما تركب من جوهرين فردين فأكثر.
And oneness in attributes, and its meaning: the non-multiplicity of attributes for the most holy essence from one genus, such as having two powers or more, or two wills or more, or two knowledges or more, contrary to one who said that it is multiplied by the multiplicity of objects, and the non-existence of an attribute for other than Him like His attribute, may He be exalted, such as another having power like His power; as for another having power not like His power, then it does not harm multiplicity of attributes for the essence. So it is an expression of negating the continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttasil) in attributes, which is the sanctified [attributes] of one genus as previously mentioned, and of negating the discrete quantity (al-kamm al-munfasil) in attributes: and it is the existence of an attribute for other than Him like His attribute, as previously mentioned. And it is difficult to conceive the continuous quantity in attributes, because it necessarily requires connection and composition from parts, and it is negated here, and it was answered that the subsistence of attributes of one genus in one essence is treated as having the status of composition. Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'and it is the existence of an attribute') applying 'quantity' to this existence is by way of applying the name of the effect to the cause, because quantity in reality is the number subsisting in God's attribute and the attribute of another similar to His attribute, assuming its existence, and with the negation of that attribute, quantity is negated. (His saying: 'the status of composition' (manzilat al-tarakub)) i.e., from individual atoms, not from attributes, because the discrete quantity is among the accidents of the body, and it is what is composed of two or more individual atoms.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines oneness of attributes, the second division of wahdaniyya. It means that there are not multiple attributes of the same genus (like two powers or two knowledges) for God, contrary to those who think multiplicity occurs due to multiple objects. It also means no other being can have an attribute that is exactly like God's attribute (e.g., power equal to God's power); having a similar but not identical attribute is not problematic. This is expressed as negating continuous quantity (multiple attributes of same genus) and discrete quantity (an attribute like God's in another). The text notes a difficulty: continuous quantity requires connection and composition, which cannot apply to attributes, but the answer is that the subsistence of multiple attributes of one genus in one essence is treated as having the status of composition—not actual physical composition but a conceptual analogy. Al-Ajhuri's gloss explains that applying 'quantity' to the existence of a similar attribute is metaphorical, because quantity is the number that would arise from two attributes. The term 'status of composition' refers to composition from individual atoms (jawahir fardah), not attributes themselves.

Key Terms
وحدانية الصفات
كم متصل
كم منفصل
تعدد الصفات
جنيس واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God has multiple attributes of the same type (e.g., two powers) due to multiple objects (e.g., creating and sustaining require separate powers).
Correction: God has only one power, one will, one knowledge, etc. Multiplicity of objects does not cause multiplicity of attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the main meaning of oneness of attributes according to the passage?
A) God has only one attribute
B) No other being has an attribute exactly like God's
C) There are no multiple similar attributes and no attribute like God's in another
D) Attributes are not distinct from essence

Key Takeaway
Oneness of attributes means God has no two attributes of the same type and no creature has an attribute identical to God's, preserving His uniqueness and perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever internally attributed ultimate power or knowledge to a human leader, celebrity, or even yourself, as if their attributes rival God's? How does the negation of discrete quantity (no creature's attribute like God's) expose that as a subtle shirk in your heart, and what practical step can you take today to redirect that ascription back to Allah alone?
?
When you think about God's will, do you imagine it as multiple or divided because He wills so many different things? How does the lesson’s refutation (will is one, objects are many) challenge that imagination, and how should this truth affect your trust that His will is singular and perfectly coherent?
?
The lesson treats multiple attributes of one genus in God as having the 'status of composition' to answer a philosophical difficulty. Are you tempted to reduce God's attributes to mere concepts or to over-analyze them? How can you balance affirming real attributes with maintaining utter awe and submission to the reality that His attributes are beyond full human comprehension?

Impropriety of Using 'Mahall' for God
Critique of using 'mahall' for God and refutation of hulul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'mahall' (locus) is inappropriate for Allah and how theologians affirm attributes without implying indwelling or accidentality.

Core Idea
Calling God a 'locus' implies indwelling, which is impossible for the Creator.

This passage from Muhammad al-Anbabi's annotations critiques the use of the term 'mahall' for Allah. It defines the concept of hulul (indwelling) and shows why it cannot apply to Allah. It also clarifies the theological position that divine attributes are not accidents nor indwelling, but qualifying specifications. Understanding this refines your grasp of tawhid and proper theological language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice the terminology: 'mahall', 'hulul', 'a'rad', 'ikhtisas na'it'.
The passage begins with a criticism of al-Fakhr's lack of etiquette.
2
Understand the definition of hulul
The passage defines hulul as the complete meeting of two existents where the second acquires a quality from the first. Identify the example of blackness in a body. Then see why this cannot apply to Allah.
Hulul in this sense implies spatial or ontological dependence.
3
Note the correct theological stance on attributes
The passage contrasts philosophers (who deny attributes) with theologians (who affirm attributes as qa'ima biha, subsisting by the essence, but not as accidents or indwelling). It ends with a prohibition of various phrases that imply indwelling.
The key phrase is 'al-ikhtisas al-na'it' (qualifying specification).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي . إساءة أدب أيضاً، وقد وقع هو فيها كالفخر؛ إذ المحلُّ يُوهِمُ ما لا يليقُ، ففي "المقاصد": إن :الحلول ملاقاة موجود لموجود بالتمام لا على سبيل المماشة والمجاوَرَةِ، بل بحيث لا يكون بينهما تباين في الوضع، ويحصُلُ للثاني صِفَةٌ من الأول، كملاقاة السَّواد للجسم، ويسمَّى الأول: حالاً، والثاني محلاً، ولا شك أن الحلول بهذا المعنى يستحيل على الله فليست ذاته محلاً، ولا صفاته حالة فيها أيضاً. وأما صفاتُ الباري؛ فالفلاسفة لا يقولون بها، والمتكلمون لا يقولون بكونها أعراضاً ولا بكونها حالة في الذات، بل قائمة بها بمعنى: الاختصاص الناعِتِ اهـ. وفي الأنوار القدسية ما نصه النورُ الثالث عشر: أنه لا يجوز أن يقال: صفاتُه تعالى حَلَّتْ في ذاته ولا ذاتُهُ محل لصفاته وإن كان مجازاً، ولا يقال: صفاتُه معه ولا مجاوِرَةٌ له ولا فيه.
Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi. Also discourtesy, and he fell into it like al-Fakhr; for the term 'locus' (al-mahall) suggests what is not fitting, for in 'al-Maqasid': 'Indeed, indwelling (hulul) is the meeting of an existent with an existent completely, not in the way of accompaniment or adjacency, but such that there is no separation in position between them, and the second acquires a quality from the first, like the meeting of blackness with a body, and the first is called 'indweller' (hall) and the second 'locus' (mahall), and there is no doubt that indwelling in this sense is impossible for God; so His essence is not a locus, nor are His attributes indwelling in it. As for the attributes of the Creator, the philosophers do not affirm them, and the theologians (mutakallimun) do not say that they are accidents (a'rad) nor that they are indwelling in the essence, rather they subsist by it in the sense of qualifying specification (al-ikhtisas al-na'it). End quote. And in 'al-Anwar al-Qudsiyya', its text: the thirteenth light: that it is not permissible to say: 'His attributes, may He be exalted, indwell in His essence', nor 'His essence is a locus for His attributes', even if metaphorically, nor is it said: 'His attributes are with Him' or 'adjacent to Him' or 'in Him'.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage, from the annotations of Muhammad al-Anbabi, is a refutation of using the term 'mahall' (locus) for God, which is a further criticism of al-Fakhr's lack of etiquette. It cites 'al-Maqasid' to define hulul (indwelling) as the complete meeting of two existents such that the second acquires a quality from the first, like blackness in a body. This is impossible for God, so His essence cannot be a locus, nor can His attributes indwell in it. It then contrasts the philosophical and theological views: philosophers deny attributes while theologians affirm that attributes are not accidents nor indwelling, but rather 'qā'ima bihā' (subsisting by it) in the sense of qualifying specification. Finally, it quotes 'al-Anwar al-Qudsiyya' stating that it is impermissible to say that attributes indwell in His essence or that His essence is a locus for attributes, even metaphorically, and also prohibits phrases like 'with Him' or 'adjacent to Him' or 'in Him'.

Correction: The passage shows that such terms imply hulul, which is a concept of spatial/ontological dependence absolutely incompatible with Allah's transcendence, even metaphorically.

Oneness in Attributes (Wahdaniyya fi al-Sifat)
Bajuri defines oneness in attributes and explains its two negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of oneness in attributes, the two types of quantum it negates, and how a potential difficulty about composition is resolved.

Core Idea
God's oneness means no two attributes of the same kind and no creature shares His exact attribute.

This lesson explores the concept of oneness in attributes (wahdaniyya fi al-sifat) as defined by Imam al-Sanusi and explained by Bajuri. It clarifies that divine oneness not only negates multiple attributes of the same genus (e.g., two powers) but also negates any creature possessing an attribute exactly like God's. The passage also addresses a subtle difficulty about composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the technical terms like الكم المتصل and الكم المنفصل.
2
Identify the two negations
The passage states that oneness in attributes negates two things: multiplicity of attributes from one genus, and any creature having an attribute exactly like God's attribute.
These correspond to continuous and discrete quantum.
3
Understand continuous and discrete quantum
Continuous quantum (al-kamm al-muttasil) would involve composition of parts from the same genus; discrete quantum (al-kamm al-munfasil) is an attribute of another being comparable to God's.
Neither applies to God.
4
Examine the difficulty and its answer
The passage notes a difficulty in depicting continuous quantum in attributes because it requires connection and composition. The answer is that multiple attributes of the same genus subsisting in one essence are treated as analogous to composition, but not actual composition.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووحدانية في الصفات ومعناها: عدم تعدُّدِ الصفات للذات الأقدس من جنس واحدٍ، كأن يكون له قُدْرَتان فأكثر، أو إرادتان فأكثر، أو علمان فأكثر، خلافاً لمن قال بتعددِ ذلك بتعدد المتعلقات، وعدم ثبوتِ صِفَةٍ لغيره كصفته تعالى، كأن يكون لغيره قدرةً كقدرته تعالى، وأما أن يكون لغيره قدرة لا كقدرته تعالى؛ فلا تعدد الصفات للذات يَضُرُّ، فهي عبارة عن نفي الكم المتصل في الصفات وهو المقدسة من جنس واحدٍ كما تقدم، وعن نفي الكم المنفصل في الصفات: وهو ثبوتُ صِفَةٍ لغيره كصفته تعالى كما تقدم أيضاً. ويُحِثَ في تصوير الكم المتصل في الصفات؛ لأنه لا بُدَّ فيه من الاتصال والتركب من أجزاء، وهو مُنتَف هنا وأجيب بأن قيام الصفات من جنس واحدٍ بالذات الواحدة مُنَزَّل منزلة التركب.
And oneness in attributes: its meaning is the non-multiplicity of attributes for the most holy Essence from one genus, such as that He has two powers or more, or two wills or more, or two knowledges or more, contrary to those who say they are multiple due to the multiplicity of objects, and the non-establishment of an attribute for other than Him like His attribute, exalted is He, such as that another has power like His power, exalted is He. As for another having power unlike His power, exalted is He, that does not harm the oneness of attributes for the Essence. So it is an expression for the negation of continuous quantum (al-kamm al-muttasil) in attributes, which is the holy [؟] from one genus as previously, and for the negation of discrete quantum (al-kamm al-munfasil) in attributes: which is the establishment of an attribute for other than Him like His attribute, exalted is He, as also previously. And there is difficulty in depicting continuous quantum in attributes, because it necessarily involves connection and composition from parts, and that is negated here. And it is answered that the subsistence of attributes from one genus in one Essence is treated as being in the position of composition.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of oneness in attributes (wahdaniyya fi al-sifat). The author begins by stating its meaning: the non-multiplicity of attributes of the same genus for the divine essence. For example, God cannot have two powers, two wills, or two knowledges. This refutes those who claim multiple attributes due to multiple objects of relation (muta'allaqat). Additionally, it negates the existence of an attribute for any other being that is exactly like God's attribute—for instance, no other being can have power equal to God's power. However, if another being has power that is not like God's, that does not negate God's oneness. The author then explains that oneness in attributes is an expression for negating two types of quantum (kamm) in attributes: continuous quantum (al-kamm al-muttasil), which would involve composition from parts of the same genus, and discrete quantum (al-kamm al-munfasil), which would involve an attribute of another being comparable to God's. The author notes a difficulty in depicting continuous quantum in attributes because it requires connection and composition, which is impossible for God. The answer given is that the subsistence of multiple attributes of the same genus in one essence is treated as analogous to composition, but it is not actual composition.

Key Terms
وحدانية في الصفات
الكم المتصل
الكم المنفصل
قيام الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that multiple attributes like power and will are multiple in genus, and thus oneness in attributes negates all multiplicity of attributes.
Correction: Oneness in attributes only negates multiplicity of attributes of the same genus (e.g., two powers), not different types of attributes (e.g., power and will are different genuses).

Checkpoint

B) Multiplicity of attributes of the same genus and any attribute like God's in creation
C) The existence of any attributes other than God's
D) The possibility of God having attributes at all

Key Takeaway
Oneness in attributes means God has no duplicate attributes of the same kind, and no creature possesses an attribute identical to His.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Oneness in Actions: Negation of Others' Agency
Bajuri defines oneness in actions and explains its two negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of oneness in actions (wahdaniyya fi al-af'al) and its two negations: discrete quantum (others performing acts independently) and continuous quantum (others sharing in God's act), as well as the exception affirming multiplicity of God's acts.

Core Idea
Oneness in actions negates any act by another or sharing in God's act, but affirms God's multiple acts.

Bajuri now turns to a key attribute: oneness in actions. This attribute establishes that no being other than God can perform any act, nor can any being share in God's act. However, we must be careful not to over-extend this negation to the multiplicity of God's own actions, which are firmly affirmed. Let’s read his precise definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then its translation. Notice how Bajuri first gives the general meaning, then unpacks it using the concepts of discrete and continuous quantum.
The passage is short but dense. Take your time.
2
Identify the core definition
Find the sentence that states the meaning of oneness in actions. It involves two negations: no act for other than Allah, and no participation of other with Allah in an act.
3
Understand the two types of quantum
Bajuri explains that the negation is of discrete quantum (an independent act by another) and continuous quantum (another sharing in the same act). Note the difference: discrete is separate acts, continuous is the same act.
Quantum here refers to 'how many'—in actions, it's about plurality.
4
Examine the exception
The passage notes a nuance: if continuous quantum is depicted as multiplicity of different acts (like creation, provision, life-giving), then it is not negated—it is affirmed. This prevents misunderstanding that oneness negates God's multiple acts.
This exception shows that oneness in actions does not deny the diversity of God's acts.
5
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Many think that 'oneness in actions' means God only does one thing. But the passage clarifies: it denies others' acts and sharing, not God's multiple acts.
This is a key point to avoid theological error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووحدانية في الأفعال، ومعناها: عَدَمُ ثبوتِ فِعْلِ لغيره تعالى، وعدم مشارَكَةِ غيره له تعالى في فعل، فهي عبارة عن نفي الكمّ المنفصل في الأفعال: وهو ثبوتُ فعل لغيره تعالى، وعن نفي الكمّ المتصل في الأفعال إِن صُوَرَ بأن يُشَارِكَهُ غيرُه تعالى في فِعْل كما قاله بعضهم وأما إن صُوّر - كما قال بعضهم - بتعدد الأفعال كالخَلْقِ والرِّزْقِ والإحياء؛ فهو ثابِتٌ لا يَصِحُ نَفْيُهُ.
And oneness in actions: its meaning is the non-establishment of an act for other than Him, exalted is He, and the non-participation of other than Him with Him, exalted is He, in an act. So it is an expression for the negation of discrete quantum (al-kamm al-munfasil) in actions: which is the establishment of an act for other than Him, exalted is He, and for the negation of continuous quantum (al-kamm al-muttasil) in actions if it is depicted as another sharing with Him in an act as some have said. But if it is depicted - as some have said - as the multiplicity of acts like creation, provision, and giving life, then it is established and its negation is not valid.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines oneness in actions (wahdaniyya fi al-af'al). The meaning is that no act can be established for any being other than God, and no other being can participate with God in any act. This negation is expressed in terms of two types of quantum in actions: discrete quantum, which would be the existence of an act for another being independent of God; and continuous quantum, which would be another being sharing with God in the same act. The author notes that if continuous quantum is depicted as sharing in the same act, it is negated. But if it is depicted as multiplicity of different acts such as creation, provision, and giving life (i.e., multiple acts performed by God), then that multiplicity is actually established and cannot be negated, because God does perform multiple acts. So oneness in actions only negates others performing acts or sharing in God's act, not the multiplicity of God's own acts.

Key Terms
وحدانية في الأفعال
الكم المنفصل في الأفعال
الكم المتصل في الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Oneness in actions negates any multiplicity in God's actions, meaning God only does one thing.
Correction: The passage clarifies that multiplicity of God's acts (like creation, provision, giving life) is actually affirmed. Oneness only negates acts by others and sharing, not the diversity of God's own acts.

Checkpoint

A) Another being sharing in the same act as God
B) Multiplicity of different acts performed by God such as creation and provision
C) Another being performing an act independently
D) Both A and C are negated

Key Takeaway
Oneness in actions negates acts by others and sharing in God's act, but affirms the multiplicity of God's own acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

List three distinct gifts you received today and consciously say, 'This is from God alone; no one else produced it.' Will you make this a daily practice to root out hidden shirk in reliance?

The Meaning of Wahdaniyya in Actions
Sanusi defines wahdaniyya in actions and explains the two types of quantity negated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of wahdaniyya in actions and how it negates two types of quantity.

Core Idea
Wahdaniyya in actions means no one else acts independently or shares in God's acts.

This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin clarifies a key attribute of Allah: oneness in actions. We often say only Allah creates, but what does that mean exactly? Sanusi breaks it down into negating two kinds of quantity—discrete and continuous—and addresses a common nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two types of quantity
The passage presents two negations: discrete quantity (no other agent) and continuous quantity (no sharing in the same act). Note how each is defined.
Think of discrete as 'separate actions by others' and continuous as 'shared participation in one action'.
3
Understand the nuance about multiplicity
Sanusi clarifies that if multiplicity means distinct actions like creation and provision, that is not denied—only independence or sharing is denied.
This prevents misunderstanding that God only does one thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووحدانية في الأفعال، ومعناها: عدم ثبوت فعل لغيره تعالى، وعدم مشاركة غيره له تعالى في فعل، فهي عبارة عن نفي الكم المنفصل في الأفعال: وهو ثبوت فعل لغيره تعالى، وعن نفي الكم المتصل في الأفعال إن صوِّرَ بأن يشاركه غيره تعالى في فعل كما قاله بعضهم، وأما إن صوِّرَ - كما قال بعضهم - بتعدد الأفعال كالخلق والرزق والإحياء؛ فهو ثابت لا يصح نفيه.
And oneness in actions, and its meaning is: the non-existence of an action of other than Him—Exalted is He—and the non-participation of other than Him with Him—Exalted is He—in an action. So it is an expression for the negation of discrete quantity (al-kamm al-munfasil) in actions, which is the existence of an action of other than Him—Exalted is He—and for the negation of continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttasil) in actions if it is pictured that other than Him participates with Him—Exalted is He—in an action, as some have said. But if it is pictured—as some have said—as a multiplicity of actions like creation, provision, and giving life, then it is established and its negation is not valid.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'wahdaniyya fi al-af'al' (oneness in actions) as used in kalam. The author explains that it consists of two aspects: first, the negation of an action belonging to any other than God (negation of discrete quantity), and second, the negation of another entity sharing with God in the same action (negation of continuous quantity). However, the author notes that if 'oneness in actions' is understood to mean that there is a multiplicity of distinct actions (like creation, provision, etc.) performed by different agents, then that kind of multiplicity does actually exist and cannot be denied; what is denied is only that another agent creates independently or shares in the same act. Thus, the definition is nuanced.

Key Terms
الوحدانية
الكم المنفصل
الكم المتصل
الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wahdaniyya in actions means that God only performs one action.
Correction: It only negates others acting independently or sharing in God's own acts, not the multiplicity of God's own actions (like creation, provision, and giving life).

Checkpoint

A) Continuous and discrete
B) Essential and accidental
C) Separate and joined
D) Temporal and eternal

Key Takeaway
Wahdaniyya fi al-af'al negates any independent action by another and any sharing in God's acts, while affirming the multiplicity of God's own actions.

?
If I truly believed that no one shares in God's actions—even in the smallest detail of my day—what would I do differently tomorrow morning? Commit to one concrete change.

Actions Belong to God Alone: Kasb and Refutation of Extremes
Al-Bajuri clarifies Sanusi's phrase and refutes Mu'tazila and Jabriyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Bajuri clarifies that the phrase 'not in His actions' only refers to God's actions, and how the orthodox view of acquisition (kasb) avoids the extremes of the Mu'tazila and the Jabriyya.

Core Idea
All actions are God's; humans only acquire them.

Al-Bajuri begins by addressing a potential misunderstanding of Sanusi's phrase 'wa la fi af'alihi'. One might think it refers to both God's and creatures' actions, but the author clarifies that the addition of 'His' specifies that it is about God's actions alone. This leads to a discussion of human agency: the servant has only acquisition (kasb), not creation. The passage then critiques two extreme sects that err in opposite directions: the Mu'tazila who claim humans create their own actions, and the Jabriyya who deny any human role.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then its literal translation. Let the text speak for itself before analyzing.
Notice the phrase 'wa la fi af'alihi' and the immediate clarification.
2
Identify the potential misinterpretation
Al-Bajuri says that one might think 'actions' includes both God's and others' actions. Why would this be a problem?
He explains that the intended meaning is limited to God's actions only.
3
Understand the concept of 'kasb'
The servant has nothing but 'acquisition' (kasb) – a middle position between creating and being compelled. Grasp this Ash'ari doctrine.
Kasb means that actions are created by God but acquired by the servant.
4
Identify the two sects and their errors
Find the Mu'tazila (servant creates own actions) and the Jabriyya (servant is compelled like a feather). Note how each extreme distorts human agency.
The Mu'tazila exaggerate; the Jabriyya deny.
5
Reflect on the middle path
Both extremes are wrong. The correct view is that God creates all actions, and humans acquire them. Why is this important for tawhid?
It preserves God's oneness in creation while affirming human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وقوله: وَلَا فِي أَفْعَالِهِ) قد يتبادرُ منه أن الأفعال قسمان: أحدهما: أفعاله تعالى والآخرُ: أفعال غيره، والقسم الأول هو الذي فيه وحدانية الأفعال، وليس ذلك مراداً، بل الإضافة لبيان الواقع؛ لأن ما وُجِدَ من الأفعال بأسرها منسوب له تعالى، ولا ثاني له فيه؛ إذ ليس للعبد فيها إلا الكسب، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم: إن العبدَ يَخْلُقُ أفعال نفسه الاختيارية بقُدْرَةٍ خَلَقَها الله فيه وخلافاً للجَبْرِيَّةِ في قولهم: بأن العبد مجبور على الفعل كالرِّيشَةِ المعلَّقَةِ في الهواء، ولا كَسْبَ له فيه أصلاً. فالمعتزلةُ فَرَّطوا حيث قالوا بأن العبدَ يَخْلُقُ فِعْلَهُ الاختياري.
And his statement: 'and not in His actions'—it may immediately occur from it that actions are two types: one of them: His actions—Exalted is He—and the other: actions of other than Him. And the first type is that in which is the oneness of actions, but that is not intended. Rather, the addition is to clarify the reality, because what is found of actions in their entirety is attributed to Him—Exalted is He—and there is no second to Him in it, because the servant has nothing but acquisition (al-kasb), contrary to the Mu'tazila in their statement that the servant creates his own voluntary actions by a power that God created in him, and contrary to the Jabriyya in their statement that the servant is compelled in action like a feather suspended in the air, with no acquisition for him in it at all. So the Mu'tazila fell short (farraṭū) when they said that the servant creates his own voluntary action.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage explains Sanusi's phrase 'wa la fi af'alihi' (and not in His actions). One might think that 'actions' here includes both God's actions and actions of others, but Bajuri clarifies that the addition of 'His' specifies God's own actions. The reason is that all actions that exist are ultimately attributable to God; the servant does not create his own actions but only acquires them (kasb). This is the orthodox Ash'ari position. The passage then critiques two extreme sects: the Mu'tazila, who claim the servant creates his own voluntary actions (which is an overstatement of human power—ifrat), and the Jabriyya, who deny any human agency, claiming the servant is completely compelled like a feather (which is an understatement—taqsir). The author concludes that the Mu'tazila 'fell short' (farraṭū) by exaggeration, though the term farraṭa usually means to neglect; here it is used in contrast to ifrat (excess), implying they erred by going to an extreme.

المعتزلة
الجبرية
الاختياري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'wa la fi af'alihi' negates any action by creatures, meaning humans have no role in their actions.
Correction: The phrase only affirms that no action occurs independently of God's power; humans acquire actions through their acquired power (kasb).

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, what do both the Mu'tazila and the Jabriyya get wrong?
A) The role of human acquisition
B) The existence of God's power
C) The meaning of oneness
D) The number of quantities

Key Takeaway
The correct understanding of divine oneness in actions requires affirming that all actions are from God while humans acquire them—avoiding both overstating and understating human agency.

?
If all actions are from God, what does it mean for your prayers? Do you approach each prayer with the awareness that even your intention to pray is a gift from Him? How can you cultivate this awareness daily?

The Four Categories of Divine Attributes
Al-Bajuri classifies the six attributes into four categories: nafsiyya, salbiyya, ma'ani, ma'nawiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of divine attributes in Ash'ari theology and how the six attributes are logically organized into these categories.

Core Idea
Divine attributes form a logical four-part classification, not a random list.

Al-Bajuri now organizes the six divine attributes into a clear classification. He explains why the first attribute is called 'nafsiyya' and how the remaining five fall into other categories. This classification provides a structured understanding of divine attributes in Ash'ari thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's words set the framework before you interpret.
Notice the grammatical note about the feminine marker.
2
Identify the four categories
Look for where the text states 'the attributes are four categories.' The categories are nafsiyya, salbiyya, ma'ani, and ma'nawiyya. Understand each based on the text.
The first category (nafsiyya) is attributed to the essence; the others are defined relative to negation or meanings.
3
Understand the 'nafsiyya' attribute
The text explains that 'nafsiyya' is so named because it accompanies the essence alone. Contrast this with 'ma'nawiyya' which accompany meanings.
Existence is the nafsiyya attribute here.
4
Reflect on the classification logic
Why are the five attributes (eternality, permanence, etc.) called 'salbiyya'? They negate qualities. The upcoming attributes (knowledge, power) are 'ma'ani' and their states are 'ma'nawiyya'.
This classification shows how attributes are grouped by their relationship to the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: فَهَذِهِ سِتَّ صِفَاتِ) أي: فهذه المذكوراتُ سِتُّ صفاتٍ، فالإشارة عائدة للمذكورات بقوله: (الوجود ... إلخ)، والفاء تفريعية أي: دالة على أن ما بعدها مُفَرَّع عما قبلها ونتيجة له وإنما لم يَأْتِ المصنف بالتاء في اسم العدد؛ لأن المعدود مؤنَّثٌ وقد ذُكِرَ، وهو حينئذ يجب تجريده منها، بخلاف ما إذا لم يُذْكَرْ، فإنه لا يجب ذلك، بل يجوز الإتيان بها فيه، ولهذا أتى بها في قوله: (والخمسة بعدها... إلخ). نعم؛ الأولى عدم الإتيان بها في هذه الحالة كما هو مقرر في محله. (قوله: الْأُولَى نَفْسِيَّةٌ) إنما نُسِبَتْ للنفس لملازمتها لها فقط، بخلاف المعنوية، فإنها ملازمة للمعاني، فلذلك نُسِبَتْ إليها. وقد عُلِمَ من كلام المصنف: أن ما تقدم من الصفات قسمان: أحدهما ـ وهو الأولى - صِفَةٌ نَفْسِيَّةٌ ، والثاني - وهو الخمسة الباقية -: صِفَاتٌ سَلْبِيَّة، وما سيأتي من الصفات قسمان أيضاً: أحدهما - وهو الوجودي منها - : صِفَاتُ المعاني، والثاني - وهو الأحوال - : صِفَاتٌ مَعْنَوِيَّةٌ. فتلخّص أن الصفاتِ أقسام أربعة.
(His saying: SO THESE ARE SIX ATTRIBUTES) meaning: these mentioned ones are six attributes, so the reference is to the mentioned ones in his saying: (Existence, etc.), and the 'fa' is connotative of consequence, indicating that what follows is derived from what preceded and results from it. The author did not bring 'ta' (feminine marker) in the number because the counted noun is feminine but has been mentioned, and in that case it is obligatory to strip the number of it, unlike when it is not mentioned, for then it is not obligatory but it is permissible to bring it, and for this reason he brought it in his saying: (and the five after it, etc.). Yes, it is better not to bring it in this case as established in its place. (His saying: THE FIRST IS NAFSIYYA) It is attributed to the essence (nafs) because of its constant accompaniment of it alone, unlike the ma'nawiyya, for they accompany the meanings, so they are attributed to them. And it is known from the author's words that what has preceded of the attributes is two divisions: one—which is the first—is a nafsiyya attribute, and the second—which is the remaining five—are salbiyya attributes. And what will come of the attributes are also two divisions: one—which is the existent ones—are attributes of ma'ani, and the second—which are the states—are ma'nawiyya attributes. So it is summarized that the attributes are four categories.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine attributes. Al-Bajuri comments on Sanusi's phrase 'so these are six attributes.' He explains that the demonstrative refers to the previously mentioned attributes (existence, etc.) and that the 'fa' indicates a logical consequence. He then addresses a grammatical point: why the author used 'sitt' without the feminine 'ta' (instead of 'sittat') before 'sifat' (feminine). The rule is that when the feminine noun is mentioned (i.e., the word 'sifat' appears), the number must be stripped of the feminine marker; when it is not mentioned, it can be kept. The author used the marker in 'alkhamsa' (the five) because the noun 'sifat' is omitted there. Next, he explains the term 'nafsiyya' (essential): it is called so because it constantly accompanies the essence itself, unlike 'ma'nawiyya' (state-like) which accompany the meanings (ma'ani). From the author's words, we learn a comprehensive classification: the attributes discussed so far (existence, etc.) are two types: the first (existence) is nafsiyya (essential), and the remaining five are salbiyya (negative). The attributes yet to be discussed (like knowledge, power, will) are also two types: the existential ones are called 'sifat al-ma'ani' (attributes of meanings), and the correlative states are called 'sifat ma'nawiyya' (state-like attributes). Thus, in total, the attributes are four categories: nafsiyya, salbiyya, ma'ani, and ma'nawiyya. The lesson provides an overview of the attribute classification system in Ash'ari theology.

Key Terms
صفات
نفسية
سلبية
معاني
معنوية
أقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'nafsiyya' means the attribute is the very essence in a philosophical sense.
Correction: In this context, 'nafsiyya' only indicates that the attribute constantly accompanies the essence, not that it is identical to it. Existence is nafsiyya because it is inseparable from the essence, not because it is the essence.

Checkpoint
How many categories of divine attributes are summarized in this passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The six divine attributes are organized into four categories: nafsiyya, salbiyya, ma'ani, and ma'nawiyya, providing a structured understanding of divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists,' do you treat that as a mere theoretical label, or does it anchor your heart in the reality that He is the only necessary being, while everything else is contingent? How would your daily choices change if you truly lived with that certainty?
?
This lesson shows that God's attributes are not random but meticulously classified. Does your knowledge of God's attributes move you to greater awe and submission, or has it become just an intellectual exercise? What one attribute can you meditate on today to increase your reliance on Him?
?
If you truly believed that God's attributes are perfect beyond any limitation (salbiyya) and active in your life (ma'ani), would you still worry about the future? What is holding you back from trusting Him completely?

The Nafsiyya Attribute: Definition and Defense
Bajuri defines the nafsiyya attribute and defends the identification with existence against objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the nafsiyya (essential) attribute, why it is identified with existence for God, and how scholars respond to the objection that essence can be conceived without existence.

Core Idea
The nafsiyya attribute is what makes an essence what it is; for God, it is existence itself.

In this passage, Bajuri presents the technical definition of the nafsiyya attribute — that without which the essence cannot be conceived — and clarifies that for God it is uniquely existence. He then addresses a key objection: if we can think of God as knowing without explicitly thinking of His existence, doesn't that contradict the definition? The commentary resolves this by distinguishing mental conception from actual existence, showing that the definition refers to external actualization.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage thoughtfully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the author's words settle before interpreting.
Notice the compact style and the dialectical structure: definition, objection, answer.
2
Identify the definition
Locate the definition of the nafsiyya attribute: 'what the essence is not intellectually conceived except through it'. Paraphrase it in your own words.
The definition is meant to apply universally, to God and to contingent things.
3
Examine the objection
The objection states that the essence can be conceived without its nafsiyya attribute, e.g., 'God is knowing' does not mention existence. Why is this a problem?
This objection tests the precision of the definition.
4
Understand the answer
The answer reinterprets 'intellectual conception' as 'actualization' (tahaqquq). The essence is not actualized without existence. The intellect cannot affirm external existence without it.
This resolves the objection by shifting from mere mental imagining to external reality.
5
Reflect on the alternative view
Consider al-Yusi's note that God has other nafsiyya attributes like majesty and beauty. Why does the mainstream view limit it to existence?
This connects to the broader theological principle of divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضابط الصفة النفسية: ما لا تتعقَّلُ الذاتُ إلا بها، وليس له تعالى صفة نفسية سوى الوجود كذا قال بعضهم. لكن في حاشية اليوسي على الكبرى (۱): أنه تعالى مخالف للحوادث بصفات نفسية كالجَلال والجمال والحِلْم ونحوها فليراجع. (قوله: وَهِيَ الْوُجُودُ) هذا إخبار بمعلوم، وإنما أَتَى به لدفع ما عسى أن يَقَعَ الأولى من تغيير بعض الكتبة بأن يُقَدِّموا القِدَمَ مثلاً على الوجود، فلا تكون هي حينئذ، وأيضاً ربما يُغْفَلُ عن صنيع المصنف فيما تقدم، فيعتقد أن الأولى هي القدم مثلاً، فلذلك نَبَّه المصنف على أن الأولى هي الوجود، وكان مقتضى ذلك أن يقول بعد قوله: (والخمسة بعدها سلبية): وهي القِدَمُ والبقاءُ إلخ، لكنه تَرَكَ ذلك لعدم الاحتياج إليه بعد التنصيص على الأولى. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وَضَابِطُ الصَّفَةِ النَّفْسِيَّةِ) أي: في حق الله وغيره، وحينئذ فهي متعددة تحتاج إلى ضابط، لا (۲) في حق الله فقط؛ لأنها حينئذ صفةً واحدةً هي الوجود، فلا يؤتى لها بضابط، ومن الصفة النفسية في حق الحادث التحيز للجرم، والقيام بالغير بالنسبة للعرض. (قوله: مَا لا تُتَعَقَّلُ الذَّاتُ إلَّا بها يرد عليه أن الذات قد تتعقل بدون الوجود، كما لو قيل مثلاً: الله ،عالم، فإن الذات حينئذ متعقلة بدون تعقل وجودها. ويجاب بأن المراد بالتعقل: التحقق، ومعلوم أن الذات لا تتحقق بدون وجودها. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبايي (قوله: مَا لَا تُتَعَقَّلُ الذَّاتُ إِلَّا بِهَا) فيه: أن الموصوف قد يتَعَقَّلُ بدون صفته النفسية، فقد تُتَعَقَّلُ الذات بدون الوجود، وقد يتعقَّلُ الجزمُ بدون التحيز، فالأولى أن يقول: ما لا تتحقق الذات خارجاً إلا بها. وأجيب بأن المعنى : لا يُصَدِّقُ العقل بوجودها خارجاً إلا بها. تأمل. (قوله: كَالْجَلَالِ وَالْجَمَالِ... إلخ) فيه: أن هذا لا يَصْدُقُ عليه تعريف النفسية، فلعله أراد بالنفسية ما ليست من قبيل المعاني والمعنوية والسلبية تدبر.
And the definition of the nafsiyya attribute: that by which the essence is not intellectually conceived except through it, and He, exalted, has no nafsiyya attribute other than existence, as some said. However, in the Hashiya of al-Yusi on al-Kubra (1): He, exalted, is different from contingent things by nafsiyya attributes such as majesty, beauty, forbearance, and the like, so let it be consulted. His saying: 'and it is existence' — this is a report of something known, and he only brought it to prevent what might happen to the first (al-ula) due to alteration by some scribes by making precedence, for example, precede existence, so then it would not be that. Also, one might be heedless of the author's work in what preceded, and think that the first is precedence, for example, so the author alerted that the first is existence. The implication would have been that after his saying: 'and the five after them are negative', he would say: they are precedence and permanence, etc., but he omitted that because there is no need for it after specifying the first. The marginal note of the scholar Ahmad al-Ujhuri: His saying: 'and the definition of the nafsiyya attribute' — that is, regarding God and other than Him. In that case, it is multiple and needs a definition, not only regarding God; because in that case it is a single attribute which is existence, so no definition is needed for it. And from the nafsiyya attribute regarding the contingent is occupation of space (tahayyuz) for a body, and standing through another for an accident. His saying: 'that by which the essence is not intellectually conceived except through it' — it is objected that the essence may be conceived without existence, as if one says for example: 'God is knowing', then the essence is conceived without conceiving its existence. And the answer is: what is meant by 'conception' (ta'aqqul) is actualization (tahaqquq), and it is known that the essence is not actualized without its existence. The glosses of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: His saying: 'that by which the essence is not intellectually conceived except through it' — there is in it: the described may be conceived without its nafsiyya attribute; the essence may be conceived without existence, and a body may be conceived without occupying space. So it is better that he say: that by which the essence is not actualized externally except through it. And it was answered: the meaning is: the intellect does not affirm its existence externally except through it. Reflect. His saying: 'such as majesty, beauty... etc.' — there is in it: this does not apply to the definition of nafsiyya, so perhaps he meant by nafsiyya what is not of the category of ma'ani, ma'nawiyya, and salbiyya. Consider.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage defines the nafsiyya attribute and addresses objections. It begins with a definition: the nafsiyya is that without which the essence is not intellectually conceived. However, some say only existence is nafsiyya for God, but al-Yusi's note mentions other attributes like majesty. Then the text explains why the author says 'and it is existence' — to prevent scribal errors and clarify that the first attribute is existence, not precedence. Al-Ujhuri then notes that the definition applies to God and other beings; for God, it is unique, but for contingents, it includes spatial occupation and dependence. An objection is raised: the essence can be conceived without existence (e.g., 'God is knowing' conceives God without explicitly conceiving existence). The answer: 'conception' here means 'actualization' — the essence cannot be actualized without existence. Al-Anbabi criticizes the wording, suggesting 'actualized externally' is better, but the answer is that it means the intellect does not affirm external existence without it. Finally, he questions whether attributes like majesty fit the definition, suggesting perhaps 'nafsiyya' is used differently. The passage thus defends the technical definition through dialectical discussion.

Key Terms
صفة نفسية
تعقل
تحقق
وجود
قدم
بقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'intellectual conception' in the definition means simply imagining the essence mentally, so that the objection (thinking of God without existence) seems to refute the definition.
Correction: The intended meaning of 'conception' is 'actualization in external reality' (tahaqquq), not mere mental imagining. The essence cannot be actualized without its nafsiyya attribute. The intellect does not affirm external existence without it.

Checkpoint
Why does the text state that 'and it is existence' was mentioned?
A) To introduce a new attribute.
B) To prevent scribes from replacing existence with precedence.

D) To show that existence is a negative attribute.

Key Takeaway
The nafsiyya attribute is defined as that without which the essence is not actualized; for God, it is existence itself, and the objection that essence can be conceived without existence is resolved by understanding 'conception' as 'actualization'.

The Definition of Essential Attribute (Sifah Nafsiyyah)
Al-Bajuri defines the essential attribute and states that only existence qualifies for Allah, with additional notes on copyist errors and an alternative view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of an essential attribute in kalam and why existence is the only attribute that qualifies for Allah according to the mainstream view.

Core Idea
An essential attribute is one without which the essence cannot be conceived — for Allah, that is only existence.

The concept of 'essential attribute' (sifah nafsiyyah) is a key building block for understanding divine attributes. In this passage, Al-Bajuri provides the standard definition and then applies it to Allah, noting that only existence meets the criterion. He also addresses a potential copyist error regarding the order of attributes and mentions an alternative view from al-Yusi. This lesson unpacks the definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the definition: 'ما لا تتعقل الذات إلا بها'. Notice that it says 'conceived' (tutaqqalu) not 'realized'.
The term 'conception' here is key and will be refined later.
2
Understand why only existence qualifies for Allah
The passage states that for Allah, only existence is essential. Ask yourself: why? Because without existence, there is no essence at all. The other attributes (like qidam) depend on existence first.
Al-Bajuri later explains that the five negative attributes come after existence in order.
3
Consider the alternative view from al-Yusi
Al-Yusi suggests that attributes like majesty and beauty are also essential. Reflect on whether this contradicts the definition or if 'essential' is used differently.
This shows there is some debate among scholars.
4
Review the explanation about why existence is listed first
Al-Bajuri explains that he explicitly states 'and it is existence' to prevent copyists from mistakenly placing qidam first. This highlights the importance of the order of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضابط الصفة النفسية: ما لا تتعقَّلُ الذاتُ إلا بها، وليس له تعالى صفة نفسية سوى الوجود كذا قال بعضهم. لكن في حاشية اليوسي على الكبرى: أنه تعالى مخالف للحوادث بصفات نفسية كالجلال والجمال والحلم ونحوها فليراجع. (قوله: وَهِيَ الْوُجُودُ) هذا إخبار بمعلوم، وإنما أَتَى به لدفع ما عسى أن يَقَعَ الأولى من تغيير بعض الكتبة بأن يُقَدِّموا القِدَمَ مثلاً على الوجود، فلا تكون هي حينئذ، وأيضاً ربما يُغْفَلُ عن صنيع المصنف فيما تقدم، فيعتقد أن الأولى هي القدم مثلاً، فلذلك نَبَّه المصنف على أن الأولى هي الوجود، وكان مقتضى ذلك أن يقول بعد قوله: (والخمسة بعدها سلبية): وهي القِدَمُ والبقاءُ إلخ، لكنه تَرَكَ ذلك لعدم الاحتياج إليه بعد التنصيص على الأولى. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وَضَابِطُ الصَّفَةِ النَّفْسِيَّةِ) أي: في حق الله وغيره، وحينئذ فهي متعددة تحتاج إلى ضابط، لا في حق الله فقط؛ لأنها حينئذ صفةً واحدةً هي الوجود، فلا يؤتى لها بضابط، ومن الصفة النفسية في حق الحادث التحيز للجرم، والقيام بالغير بالنسبة للعرض. (قوله: مَا لا تُتَعَقَّلُ الذَّاتُ إلَّا بها يرد عليه أن الذات قد تتعقل بدون الوجود، كما لو قيل مثلاً: الله عالم، فإن الذات حينئذ متعقلة بدون تعقل وجودها. ويجاب بأن المراد بالتعقل: التحقق، ومعلوم أن الذات لا تتحقق بدون وجودها. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبايي (قوله: مَا لَا تُتَعَقَّلُ الذَّاتُ إِلَّا بِهَا) فيه: أن الموصوف قد يتَعَقَّلُ بدون صفته النفسية، فقد تُتَعَقَّلُ الذات بدون الوجود، وقد يتعقَّلُ الجزمُ بدون التحيز، فالأولى أن يقول: ما لا تتحقق الذات خارجاً إلا بها. وأجيب بأن المعنى : لا يُصَدِّقُ العقل بوجودها خارجاً إلا بها. تأمل. (قوله: كَالْجَلَالِ وَالْجَمَالِ... إلخ) فيه: أن هذا لا يَصْدُقُ عليه تعريف النفسية، فلعله أراد بالنفسية ما ليست من قبيل المعاني والمعنوية والسلبية تدبر.
The definition of the essential attribute (sifah nafsiyyah): that without which the essence is not conceived, and He, exalted, has no essential attribute except existence – thus said some. But in the Hashiyah of al-Yusi on al-Kubra: that He, exalted, is different from contingents by essential attributes such as majesty (jalal), beauty (jamal), forbearance (hilm) and the like, so let it be referred to. (His saying: 'and it is existence') This is an announcement of something known; he only brought it to repel what might happen to the first [attribute] by the alteration of some copyists by advancing, for example, pre-eternity (qidam) over existence, so that it would not be it [i.e., the first]. Also, perhaps one may be heedless of the author's practice in what preceded, and thus believe that the first is pre-eternity, for example. Therefore, the author alerted that the first is existence. And the requirement of that would be that he say after his saying: 'and the five after it are negative': 'they are pre-eternity and perpetuity etc.', but he left that due to no need for it after explicit designation of the first. The Hashiyah of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'And the definition of the essential attribute') i.e., with respect to God and other than Him. In that case, it is multiple and needs a definition, not only with respect to God; because in that case it is one attribute, existence, and thus no definition is given for it. Among the essential attributes in the case of the contingent are spatial occupation (tahayyuz) for a body, and inherence in another for an accident. (His saying: 'That without which the essence is not conceived' – it is objected that the essence may be conceived without existence, as if it is said, for example, 'God is knowing', for the essence then is conceived without conceiving its existence. And it is answered that what is meant by 'conception' is realization, and it is known that the essence is not realized without its existence. The Taqrirat of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His saying: 'That without which the essence is not conceived') in it: that the described may be conceived without its essential attribute; the essence may be conceived without existence, and the body may be conceived without spatial occupation. So it would be better for him to say: that without which the essence is not realized externally except with it. And it is answered that the meaning is: the intellect does not affirm its existence externally except with it. Ponder. (His saying: 'like majesty and beauty etc.') in it: that this does not apply to the definition of the essential attribute; perhaps he meant by 'essential' what is not of the category of accidents (ma'ani), relational (ma'nawiyyah), and negative (salbiyyah). Ponder.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage defines the 'essential attribute' (sifah nafsiyyah) and addresses a key objection. The definition given is: an attribute such that the essence (dhat) is not conceived (tu'taqal) without it. For Allah, only existence (wujud) qualifies, according to the main view. However, a note from al-Yusi's commentary suggests that attributes like majesty and beauty are also essential in the sense of describing His incomparability. The commentator then explains why the author explicitly states that existence is the first attribute: to prevent copyists from mistakenly placing pre-eternity (qidam) first, and to clarify that the five negative attributes come later. Al-Ajhuri's gloss expands the scope: the definition applies to both God and creatures, but for God there is only one essential attribute (existence), whereas for bodies and accidents there are multiple. The main objection is that the essence can be conceived without existence—for example, when one says 'God is knowing', the essence is conceived without explicitly conceiving existence. The answer reinterprets 'conception' as 'realization' (tahaqquq): the essence is not realized externally without existence. Al-Anbabi's Taqrirat adds another objection: even the body can be conceived without spatial occupation. He suggests a better phrasing: 'that without which the essence is not realized externally except with it.' The answer given is that the intellect does not affirm (yusaddiq) its external existence except with that attribute. Finally, the mention of jalal and jamal is discussed: these do not fit the definition of essential attribute, so perhaps 'nafsiyyah' here means what is not of the categories of attributes of meanings (ma'ani), relational (ma'nawiyyah), or negative (salbiyyah). The passage thus refines the concept of essential attribute and resolves potential conceptual confusions.

Key Terms
الصفة النفسية
التعقل
الوجود
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'essential attribute' refers to any attribute that is important or necessary for the essence, but the precise definition requires that the essence cannot be conceived without it, which only existence satisfies for Allah.
Correction: The definition is strict: 'that without which the essence is not conceived.' Attributes like pre-eternity, though necessary, are not essential because the essence can be conceived without them (i.e., one can think of God's essence without explicitly thinking of pre-eternity, but not without existence).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of an essential attribute (sifah nafsiyyah)?
A) An attribute that is necessarily true of God
B) An attribute without which the essence is not conceived
C) An attribute that is not dependent on anything else
D) An attribute that is unique to God

Key Takeaway
The essential attribute is defined as that without which the essence cannot be conceived, and for Allah, only existence meets this criterion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Without existence, the essence of Allah is not conceived. Reflect: In your daily life, do you truly rely on Allah's existence as the foundation of your faith, or do you sometimes treat attributes like His mercy or power as more immediate? How would your relationship with Allah change if you consistently grounded all His attributes in His essential existence?
?
The author warns that copyists might mistakenly advance pre-eternity over existence, showing that small errors can distort theology. What small 'copyist errors' might you be making in your understanding of Allah? What steps will you take to verify that your concepts are correctly ordered according to revelation?
?
Al-Yusi suggests that attributes like majesty and beauty are also essential in describing Allah's incomparability. How does contemplating these attributes alongside existence deepen your awe of Allah? What one attribute of Allah will you consciously reflect on today to increase your reverence?

Resolving the Objection: Can the Essence be Conceived Without Existence?
Al-Bajuri presents and resolves the objection that the essence can be conceived without existence, with additional glosses refining the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the essence can be conceived without existence, and how kalam scholars resolve it by distinguishing between conception and external realization.

Core Idea
The objection is answered by reinterpreting 'conception' as 'realization' — the intellect does not affirm external existence without the attribute.

A key objection to the definition of essential attribute is that one can supposedly conceive of the essence without existence, as when saying 'God is knowing' without explicitly thinking of existence. Al-Bajuri addresses this by clarifying that what is meant by 'conception' is actual realization (tahaqquq). This lesson explores that objection, the answer, and further refinements from other scholars.

Guided Path
1
State the objection clearly
The objection says: we can conceive of the essence without existence because we say 'God is knowing' without saying 'God exists'. This seems to contradict the definition. Read the Arabic objection.
The example is from the Taqrirat of al-Anbabi.
2
Understand the answer: conception means realization
The answer is that 'tutaqqalu' here means 'tahaqquq' (realization). The essence is not realized externally without existence. So the definition holds.
This is a common hermeneutic move in kalam: technical terms have precise meanings.
3
Consider the further objection from al-Anbabi
Al-Anbabi notes that even a body can be conceived without spatial occupation (tahayyuz). He suggests a better phrasing: 'that without which the essence is not realized externally except with it.'
The answer given is that the intellect does not affirm external existence without that attribute.
4
Reflect on the mention of jalal and jamal
The passage ends with a remark that jalal and jamal do not fit the definition of essential attribute, so the term 'nafsiyyah' there might be used in a different sense (non-accidental, non-relational, non-negative).
This shows careful terminological precision.

Commentary
This passage defines the 'essential attribute' (sifah nafsiyyah) and addresses a key objection. The definition given is: an attribute such that the essence (dhat) is not conceived (tu'taqal) without it. For Allah, only existence (wujud) qualifies, according to the main view. Al-Ajhuri's gloss expands the scope: the definition applies to both God and creatures, but for God there is only one essential attribute (existence), whereas for bodies and accidents there are multiple. The main objection is that the essence can be conceived without existence—for example, when one says 'God is knowing', the essence is conceived without explicitly conceiving existence. The answer reinterprets 'conception' as 'realization' (tahaqquq): the essence is not realized externally without existence. Al-Anbabi's Taqrirat adds another objection: even the body can be conceived without spatial occupation. The answer given is that the intellect does not affirm (yusaddiq) its external existence except with that attribute. The passage thus refines the concept of essential attribute and resolves potential conceptual confusions.
